In characteristic 0, however, our intuition is correct: Theorem IV-4.IfC‚äāP 2 K is any nonsingular plane curve of degreed>1 over an algebraically closed fieldKof characteristic zero, then not every point ofCis a flex (so that in particularChas exactly3d(d‚ąí2)flexes, counting multiplicity).
Proof.See for example Hartshorne [1977, Chapter IV, Exercise 2.3e] or Griffiths and Harris [1978, Chapter 2, Section 4].
Flexes of multiplicitym>1 certainly can occur on nonsingular curves.
This naturally raises the question of whether, on a general curve, all the flexes are simple (that is, have multiplicity 1). In fact, this is the case: Exercise IV-5.LetKbe an algebraically closed field. Fix an integerd>2 and letB=P N K the projective space parametrizing plane curvesC‚äāP 2 K of degreed. Show that for a general point [C]‚ąąB‚ÄĒ that is, for all points [C] in a dense open set inB‚ÄĒ all the flexes on the corresponding curve C‚äāP 2 K are simple.

IV.1.2   Flexes on Singular Curves Interesting new questions arise when we consider singular curves. First of all, every singular point is a flex: Exercise IV-7.LetC‚äāP 2 K be a plane curve. Show that all singular points ofCare flexes.
Hint:either exhibit a line through a singular pointpofCwith intersec- tion multiplicity 3 or more by looking at the tangent cone toCatp(that is, expandingfaroundpand taking a component of the zero locus of the quadratic term); or use Exercise IV-2 and show that the Hessian vanishes 
156IV.  Classical Constructions atpby observing thatXtimes the first column of the Hessian determinant, plusYtimes the second column, plusZtimes the third, vanishes atp.
There are two sorts of questions about flexes on singular curves. First, we can consider curvesCwith isolated singularities and no line components, so that the HessianC  will still meetCin a zero-dimensional scheme őď, and thusCwill have a finite number of flexes; we ask for the number of flexes supported at nonsingular points ofC.To find this number, we simply have to find the degree of the part of the scheme őď whose support is contained in the singular locusC sing and subtract this from 3d(d‚ąí2). It turns out that this has a nice answer in particular cases, two of which are expressed in the following exercise.

Thus, over an algebraically closed field, the number of nonsingular flexes of a plane curve of degreednot containing any lines and having as singu- laritiesőīordinary nodes andőļcusps is 3d(d‚ąí2)‚ąí6őī‚ąí8őļ.
This is an example of the classicalPl Őąucker formulasfor plane curves.
Exercise IV-9.Verify that ifCis reducible (again assuming no com- ponent ofCis a line), we can get the same answer by considering the components ofCindividually.
IV.1.3   Curves with Multiple Components A very different sort of question emerges when we consider curves with multiple components, for example the curve defined by a powerF=G m of a polynomialG(X, Y, Z). Of course, for such a curveCthe schemeF C of flexes is positive-dimensional, and typically not that interesting. Rather, the interesting questions arise when we considerfamilies of curvesspecializing to such a multiple curve. We ask: in such a family, where do the flexes go?
To give just an example of such a problem, consider the case of a nonsin- gular quartic plane curve degenerating to a double conic in a linear family.
LetKbe an algebraically closed field of characteristic zero and consider a curveCover the schemeB=A 1 K =SpecK[t]. SupposeU=U(X, Y, Z)is an irreducible quadric polynomial andG=G(X, Y, Z) any quartic poly- nomial such that the curvesV(U) andV(G)‚äāP 2 K intersect transversely.
 IV.1 Flexes of Plane Curves157 Consider the familyŌÄ:C‚ÜíA 1 K of quartic plane curves given by the equationF=U 2 +tG= 0 ‚ÄĒ that is, the scheme ŌÄ:C= ProjK[t][X, Y, Z]/  U(X, Y, Z) 2 +tG(X, Y, Z)  ‚äāProjK[t][X, Y, Z]=P 2 B ‚ąí‚ÜíB.

LetF  be the closure ofF ‚ąó inP 2 B , and letF  0 be the fiber ofF  over the origin. SinceBis one-dimensional and nonsingular,F  will be flat over all ofB; it follows in particular thatF  0 ‚äāC 0 ‚äāP 2 K has dimension zero and degree 24 overK. We may think ofF  0 as the ‚Äúlimiting position‚ÄĚ of the 24 flexes of the nearby nonsingular curvesC őľ asőľapproaches zero.
Thus, the naive question, ‚Äúwhere do the flexes of a plane quartic go when the quartic degenerates into a double conic?‚ÄĚ translates into the precise problem: determine the flat limitF  0 , and in particular its support.
What makes this tricky is that the schemeF  0 is not the fiber ofFover the origin. Rather,Fwill have two components: one, the closureF  ofF ‚ąó consisting of the ‚Äúreal‚ÄĚ flexes and their limits, and the other supported on the conicV(t, U) in the special fiberŌÄ ‚ąí1 ((t)) =P 2 K ofP 2 B

In other words: Proposition IV-10.The schemeF  0 is supported at the eight pointst= U=G=0of intersection of the conicV(U)and the quarticV(G)in the planeV(t). At each point, it consists of a curvilinear scheme of degree3, tangent to, but not contained in, the conicV(U).
One aspect of this answer is that any closed point of the reduced curve (C 0 ) red could be a limit of flexes of nonsingular curves for a suitable family of curvesC őľ tending toC 0
. This is a general phenomenon; in fact, every point of a multiple component of a curve is a limit of flexes of nearby nonsingular curves.
The phenomenon described in this example is fairly general. The follow- ing exercises give two generalizations.

There is one case, other than that of a curve with multiple components, in which a plane curve over a field of characteristic 0 may have a positive- dimensional scheme of flexes: that of a curveC‚äāP 2 K containing a line.
We may ask in this setting the analogous question: given a family of plane curves specializing to one containing a line ‚ÄĒ for eample, withKandB as above, the familyŌÄ:C‚ÜíBof plane curves of degreedgiven by the equationLF+tG= 0 forL,FandGgeneral polynomials of degrees 1, d‚ąí1 anddrespectively ‚ÄĒ where do the flexes of the general fiber ofC‚ÜíB go? The answer turns out to be in some ways more subtle than that in the case of multiple components; we will not describe it here, for lack of some necessary language, but will mention that (as the reader may verify) the location of the limiting flexes on the lineV(L)isnotthe intersection of V(L) withV(G).
To conclude this section, here is an amusing aspect of the geometry of flexes on plane cubics.
Exercise IV-13.Consider a nonsingular plane cubic curveC‚äāP 2 R over the real numbers. Show that the scheme of flexes will consist, for some pair of integersaandbwitha+2b=9,ofapoints with residue fieldRandb points with residue fieldC. Deduce in particular thatCmust have a real flex.
In fact, the numberain this problem is 3. For the pleasure of the reader familiar with the classical theory of elliptic curves, we sketch the argument.
Part of it is simple: the exclusion of 5 and 7 follows from the existence of a group law on the set of points ofCwith residue fieldR, in terms of which the flexes with residue fieldRform a subgroup of the groupZ/(3)√óZ/(3) of the 9 flexes ofC√ó R SpecC. To see thata= 3, we observe that the R-rational points ofCform a compact real one-dimensional Lie group, and hence is isomorphic toS 1 √óGwhereGis a finite group. For degree reasons, Gcan have cardinality at most 2.
More generally, ifKis any field andC‚äāP 2 K a nonsingular plane cubic, the number of flex pointsp‚ąąCwith residue fieldKwill be 0, 1, 3 or 9. This phenomenon is strictly limited to cubics, however: it follows from 
162IV.  Classical Constructions Harris [1979] that for anyd‚Č•4 and any numberőīwith 0‚Č§őī‚Č§3d(d‚ąí2), őī=3d(d‚ąí2)‚ąí1, there exists a fieldKand a nonsingular plane curve C‚äāP 2 K of degreedwhose scheme of flexes contains exactlyőīpoints with residue fieldK.
In Chapter V we‚Äôll discuss another object classically associated to a plane curveC‚äāP 2 S : itsdual curveC ‚ąó ‚äā(P 2 S ) ‚ąó
. We‚Äôll encounter many phenom- ena analogous to those we have just discovered.
IV.2   Blow-ups Blowing up is a basic tool in classical algebraic geometry. It is used to resolve singularities, to resolve the indeterminacy of rational maps, and to relate birational varieties to one another. Saying that one variety is a blow-up of another along a given subvariety expresses a relationship that is simultaneously close enough to relate the structure of the two intimately, and flexible enough that it is a very common ingredient in the expression of maps between varieties. In this section, we will extend the definition to the category of schemes, defining the notion of the blow-up of an arbitrary (Noetherian) scheme along an arbitrary closed subscheme.
Generalizing the definition of blow-ups in this way actually serves two purposes. First there is the expected benefit: blowing up schemes other than varieties is useful for the same reason blowing up varieties is, that is, for resolving singularities or relating two birational schemes (for example, we will blow up arithmetic schemes in Section IV.2.4).
In addition we will see that, even in the context of maps between varieties, the language of schemes ‚ÄĒ specifically, being able to talk about blow-ups of a varietyXalong possibly nonreduced subschemesY‚äāX‚ÄĒ represents a highly useful extension of the concept. For example, we will illustrate this in Section IV.2.3 below, where we extend the classical description of nonsingular quadric surfaces as blow-ups of the plane to quadric cones, using this generalized notion of blowing up. Likewise, in Section IV.2.3 we will see a naturally occurring map of varieties that turns out to be a blow-up along a subscheme. These examples are in fact not special: when we broaden the definition of ‚Äúblow-up‚ÄĚ in this way, it turns out that any projective birational morphism of varieties is a blow-up! This is proved in Hartshorne [1977, Theorem II.7.17].
IV.2.1   Definitions and Constructions For the following, we will assume the reader is familiar with the basic notion of blowing up in the classical context, that is, blowing up varieties along nonsingular subvarieties. (This material is amply covered in, among others, Harris [1995], Hartshorne [1977, Chapter 1], and Shafarevich [1974].) In the 
IV.2 Blow-ups163 simplest circumstances ‚ÄĒ for example, blowing up a reduced point in the affine plane over an algebraically closed fieldK‚ÄĒ a blow up map may be described exactly as it is classically. We start by reviewing an example, the blow-up of the plane at the origin, to see how the classical construction of the blow-up via gluing may be carried out as well in the category of schemes over a field. Generalizing this to the definition of the blow-up Bl Y (X)‚ÜíXof an arbitrary schemeXalong an arbitrary closed subscheme Y‚äāXis simply a matter of expressing this standard construction in a sufficiently natural way. In the following subsection we will give several characterizations of blow-ups in general: a definition, two constructions, and a further description in some special cases, such as the blow-up of a scheme along a regular subscheme (Definition IV-15).
An Example: Blowing up the Plane.
Example IV-14.We start with the blow-upZof the origin in the affine planeA 2 K =SpecK[x, y]  over a fieldK.This can  be  most  concretely described as the union of two open sets, each isomorphic toA 2 K : we let U  =SpecK[x  ,y  ]  andU  =SpecK[x  ,y  ],  and  consider  the  maps ŌÜ  :U  ‚ÜíA 2 K andŌÜ  :U  ‚ÜíA 2 K dual to the ring homomorphisms (ŌÜ  ) # :K[x, y]‚ąí‚ÜíK[x  ,y  ] x‚ąí‚Üíx  y‚ąí‚Üíx  y  and(ŌÜ  ) # :K[x, y]‚ąí‚ÜíK[x  ,y  ] x‚ąí‚Üíx  y  y‚ąí‚Üíy 
.
The mapŌÜ  gives an isomorphism between the open subsets U  x =SpecK[x  ,y  ,x ‚ąí1 ]   andU x =SpecK[x, y, x ‚ąí1 ], and similarlyŌÜ  gives an isomorphism between the open subsetsU  y = SpecK[x  ,y  , 1 y  ] andU y =SpecK[x, y, y ‚ąí1 ]. In particular, they give iso- morphisms of the inverse images U  xy =SpecK[x  ,y  ,x ‚ąí1 ,y ‚ąí1 ]   andU  xy =SpecK[x  ,y  ,x ‚ąí1 ,y ‚ąí1 ] of the intersectionU xy =U x ‚ą©U y =SpecK[x, y, x ‚ąí1 ,y ‚ąí1 ]. We can thus identify the open setsU  xy ‚äāU  andU  xy ‚äāU  , and so glue togetherU  andU  to obtain a scheme Z=U  ‚ą™U  =SpecK[x  ,y  ] 
 U  xy ‚ąľ = U  xy SpecK[x  ,y  ], where the isomorphismU  xy ‚ąľ = U  xy is given by the ring homormorphism K[x  ,y  ,x ‚ąí1 ,y ‚ąí1 ]‚ąí‚ÜíK[x  ,y  ,x ‚ąí1 ,y ‚ąí1 ] x  ‚ąí‚Üíx  y  y  ‚ąí‚Üíx ‚ąí1
.
We call the unionZ,with its structure morphismŌÜ:Z‚ÜíA 2 K , theblow-up ofA 2 K at the origin. The inverse imageE=ŌÜ ‚ąí1 (0,0)‚äāZof the origin 
164IV.  Classical Constructions is isomorphic toP 1 K (this is called theexceptional divisorof the blow-up), whileŌÜis an isomorphism everywhere else, that is,Z\E ‚ąľ = A 2 K \{(0,0)}.
One way to think of this construction is to observe that the coordinate rings of the open subsets of the blow-up are enlarged to include the ratios y  =y/xandx  =x/yrespectively. This has a number of consequences. For one thing, the pair of functionsx, yonA 2 K define a mapf:A 2 K \{(0,0)}‚Üí P 1 K on the complement of the origin: in classical language, this is the map (a, b)‚Üí[a, b], or in modern terms it is the map associated to the surjection O‚äēO‚ÜíOgiven by (f, g)‚Üíxf+yg. This map cannot be extended to a regular map on all ofA 2 K ; but if we composefwith the isomorphism Z\E ‚ąľ = A 2 K \{(0,0)}, we see it does extend to a regular map on all ofZ.
This is because the ideal generated by the (pullbacks of the) functionsx andyis locally principal onZ(and generated by a nonzerodivisor), so that wherexandyhave common zeroes we can simply divide the homogeneous vector [x, y] by their common factor to extend the map. Another effect of the enlarged coordinate rings in the blow-up is to separate the lines through the origin. That is, ifLandL  are distinct lines through the origin inA 2 K , the preimages ofL\{(0,0)}andL  \{(0,0)}have doisjoint closures, as shown in the picture (these are just the fibers of the mapf).
By the same token, if we have a curveC‚äāA 2 K with a node at the origin, the inverse image of the complement of the origin inCis nonsingular inZ, meeting the exceptional divisor at two points.
Definition of Blow-ups in General.We will use these observations as starting points in generalizing the definition of a blow-up to that of an arbitrary scheme along an arbitrary subscheme. The essential fact is that, in the blow-upŌÜ:Bl Y (X)‚ÜíXof a schemeXalong the subscheme 
IV.2 Blow-ups165 Y‚äāX,the inverse image ofYis locally principal. To formalize this, we start with a definition: Definition IV-15.LetXbe any scheme,Y‚äāXa subscheme. We say thatYis anCartier subschemeinXif it is locally the zero locus of a single nonzerodivisor; that is, if for allp‚ąąXthere is an affine neighborhood U=SpecAofpinXsuch thatY‚ą©U=V(f)‚äāUfor some nonzerodivisor f‚ąąA. More generally, we say thatYis aregular subschemeif it is locally the zero locus of a regular sequence of functions onX.
Definition IV-16.LetXbe any scheme,Y‚äāXa subscheme. Theblow- up ofXalongY, denotedŌÜ:Bl Y (X)‚ÜíX,is the morphism toXcharac- terized by these properties: (1)  The inverse imageŌÜ ‚ąí1 (Y)ofYis a Cartier subscheme in Bl Y (X).
(2)ŌÜ:Bl Y (X)‚ÜíXis universal with respect to this property; that is, if f:W‚ÜíXis any morphism such thatf ‚ąí1 (Y) is a Cartier subscheme inZ,there is a unique morphismg:W‚ÜíBl Y (X) such thatf=ŌÜ‚ó¶g.
The inverse imageE=ŌÜ ‚ąí1 (Y)ofYin Bl Y Xis called theexceptional divisorof the blow-up, andYthecenterof the blow-up.
It is clear that these properties uniquely characterize the blow-upŌÜ: Bl Y (X)‚ÜíXof a scheme along a subscheme. It is less clear that the blow-up exists, but we shall soon see that it does.
In the affine case the blow-up can be realized in a very simple way as the closure of the graph of a morphism, and we describe this construction first. We start by generalizing the construction of Example IV-14 to the blow-up at the origin of affine space over an arbitrary ring.
Example IV-17.LetAbe any ring and letA n A =SpecA[x 1 ,...,x n ].

 170IV.  Classical Constructions The Blowup as Proj.We have now proved the existence of the blow-up of an affine scheme along a closed subscheme. We could at this point deduce the existence of blow-ups in general by gluing. However, there is a more elegant construction of blow-ups via global Proj, which accomplishes this in one fell swoop.
Theorem IV-23.LetXbe a scheme andY‚äāXa closed subscheme. Let I=I Y,X ‚äāO X be the ideal sheaf ofYinX.IfAis the sheaf of graded O X -algebras A= ‚ąě  n=0 I n =O X ‚äēI‚äēI 2 ‚äē¬∑¬∑¬∑ (where thek-th summand is taken to be thek-th graded piece ofA), then the schemeProj(A)‚ÜíXis the blow-up ofXalongY.
Remark.This construction often leads to notational confusion: iff‚ąą O X (U) is a regular function vanishing onY, the symbol ‚Äúf‚ÄĚ could a priori be used to denote either the section ofA 0 =O X or the section ofA 1 =I‚ÄĒ two different sections ofA. We will use this notation in the proof below.
Proof.We have to show that the morphism ŌÜ:B= Proj(A)‚ÜíX satisfies the two conditions that characterize a blow-up: that the preimage ŌÜ ‚ąí1 YofYinBis Cartier, and that any morphismf:Z‚ÜíXwithf ‚ąí1 Y Cartier factors uniquely throughB. We will writeIfor the idealI Y ofY inX.

. This is the truncation of the graded module A(1) =O‚äēI‚äēI 2 ‚äē¬∑¬∑¬∑ 
IV.2 Blow-ups171 (again the termI d+1 occurs in degreed.) Thus by Exercise III-46,IO B = O B (1) is invertible.
It remains to show that iff:Z‚ÜíXis a map such thatf ‚ąí1 Yis Cartier, thenffactors uniquely throughB. We will assume for simplicity thatIis coherent. We will realizeBas a closed subscheme ofP(I) = Proj Sym(I) and produce the desired map fromZtoBby giving a natural map from ZtoP(I) whose image is contained inB.

Becausef ‚ąí1 Yis Cartier, its idealI¬∑O Z is invertible. Thus the natural surjection f ‚ąó I=I‚äó O X O Z ‚ÜíI¬∑O Z corresponds as in Theorem III-44 to a mapőĪ:Z‚ÜíP(I). Both the fact thatf=ŌÜőĪand the uniqueness ofőĪfollow as well from the density ofZ\f ‚ąí1 YinZand the last sentence of Exercise III-24.
We assumed for simplicity that the ideal sheafIwas coherent (and not merely quasicoherent); the quasicoherent case could be handled by means of a straightforward generalization of III-44.
Blowing up gives us another way to interpret the projectivized tangent cone to a scheme, which we will use later in this section.
Exercise IV-24.Show that the exceptional divisor in the blow-up Bl p (X) of a schemeXat a pointp‚ąąXis the projectivized tangent conePTC p (X) toXatp.
Blow-ups along Regular Subschemes.As we mentioned before the statement of Theorem IV-23, the construction of a blow-up may not be as explicit in practice as it appears. The reason is that, even given explicit equations for a schemeXand a subschemeY,it may not be obvious how to express theRees algebra A= ‚ąě  n=0 t n I n Y,X ‚äāO X [t] in terms of explicit generators and relations. (The generators are clear, assuming we know locally generators of the ideal sheafI Y,X ; it‚Äôs knowing when we have found all the relations that may be tricky.) There is, however, one circumstance in which the Rees algebra has a nice description: when the subschemeY‚äāXis a regular subscheme. We will state the result first in caseYhas codimension two.
 172IV.  Classical Constructions Proposition IV-25.LetAbe a Noetherian ring andx, y‚ąąA; letBbe the Rees algebra B=A[xt, yt]‚äāA[t].
Ifx, y‚ąąAis a regular sequence, then B ‚ąľ = A[X, Y]/(yX‚ąíxY) via the mapX‚Üíxt,Y‚Üíyt.
Proof.First we invertxand setX  =x ‚ąí1 X‚ąąA[x ‚ąí1 ][X, Y]. The element yX  ‚ąíY‚ąąA[x ‚ąí1 ][X, Y]=A[x ‚ąí1 ][X  ,Y] generates the kernel of the map A[x ‚ąí1 ][X  ,Y]‚ąí‚ÜíA[x ‚ąí1 ][t], X  ‚ąí‚Üít, Y‚ąí‚Üíyt.
Since (yX‚ąíxY)=(yX  ‚ąíY) in the ringA[x ‚ąí1 ][X, Y], it suffices to show thatxis a nonzerodivisor moduloyX‚ąíxYinA[X, Y]. Notice that, in the other order,yX‚ąíxYis obviously a nonzerodivisor modulox‚ÄĒ it‚Äôs congru- ent toyX, the product of two nonzerodivisors! In general, a permutation of a regular sequence is not a regular sequence, but in this setting, as in many others, it is; see Eisenbud [1995, Section 17.1].
In our case we may argue as follows: To show thatxis a nonzerodivisor moduloyX‚ąíxYwe must show that M:= (yX‚ąíxY):(x) (yX‚ąíxY) =0, where (yX‚ąíxY):(x) denotes the ideal{f‚ąąA[X, Y]|fx‚ąą(yX‚ąíxY)}.
Note thatyX‚ąíxY‚Č°yXmodulox,so(x, yX‚ąíxY) is a regular sequence inA[X, Y]. Further,yX‚ąíxYis clearly a nonzerodivisor (to annihilate it, a polynomialf(X, Y) would have to have leading term inXannihilating x, which is a nonzerodivisor by hypothesis). It follows that the quotientM is isomorphic to the first homology group of the Koszul complex 0  A  ‚ąíx yX‚ąíxY   A 2 (yX‚ąíxY    x)  A.
By the same argument, this group is isomorphic to (x):(yX‚ąíxY) (x) , which is 0 sincex, yX‚ąíxYis a regular sequence. (For a more leisurely treatment of this last argument, see Eisenbud [1995, Section 17.1].) 
IV.2 Blow-ups173 The heart of the proof above is the statement that ifIis generated by a regular sequence of length 2, then the Rees algebra A‚äēI‚äēI 2 ‚äē¬∑¬∑¬∑ is isomorphic to the symmetric algebra Sym A (I) and this in turn is defined by the determinant of the 2√ó2 matrix  xy XY 
.

Another surprisingly rich example is the blow-up of a quadric cone of dimension 3.

This is the content of the following exercises, which will require one further definition: Definition IV-34.LetKbe an algebraically closed field of characteristic not equal to 2 andXany scheme overK.We say that a pointp‚ąąXis an ordinary double pointif the formal completion of the local ringO X,p is ňÜ O X,p ‚ąľ = K[[x 1 ,...,x n ]]/(x 2 1 +x 2 2 +¬∑¬∑¬∑+x 2 n ).
 178IV.  Classical Constructions For example, an ordinary double point of a curve is what we have been calling a node. .

IV.2.3   Blow-ups along Nonreduced Schemes Up to now, we have dealt only with examples of blow-ups Bl Y X‚ÜíXin which all three objects involved ‚ÄĒ the original schemeX,the subscheme Yand the blow-up Bl Y X‚ÄĒ are varieties. In the remaining two parts of this section, we will consider the behavior of blow-ups in the more general setting of schemes, giving examples first of blow-ups along non-reduced subschemes of a schemeX,and then of blow-ups of arithmetic schemes.
We will start here by giving some examples of blow-ups of varieties along nonreduced subschemes.
Blowing Up a Double Point.LetX=A 2 K =SpecK[x, y], and let őď‚äāA 2 K be the subscheme given by the idealI=(x 2 ,y). The blow-up Z=Bl őď (A 2 K ) will be ProjA, whereAis the ring A=K[x, y]‚äēI‚äēI 2 ‚äē¬∑¬∑¬∑ By Proposition IV-25, we can also writeZas Z= ProjK[x, y][A, B]/(yA‚ąíx 2 B) which is covered by the open sets U A =SpecK[x, y][b]/(y‚ąíx 2 b) and U B =SpecK[x, y][a]/(ya‚ąíx 2 ) wherea=A/Bandb=B/A.
We can see immediately some differences between this scheme and the ordinary blow-up ofA 2 K at the origin. For one thing, though the fiber of 
180IV.  Classical Constructions each over the origin is isomorphic toA 1 K , the schemeZ=Bl őď (A 2 K )is singular at one pointP(the pointa=x=y=0inU B ), while the ordinary blow-up is nonsingular.
We can see more if we expressZin terms of blow-ups with reduced centers. Briefly, the ‚Äúrecipe‚ÄĚ forZin classical language is this (see figure below): first, letZ 1 be the blow-up ofA 2 K at the origin; letE‚äāZ 1 be the exceptional divisor, that is, the inverse image of the origin. LetPbe the point ofElying on the proper transform of thex-axis ‚ÄĒ that is, the closure of the preimage of thex-axis inZ 1 \E.LetZ 2 be the blow-up of Z 1 atP; letF‚äāZ 2 be the exceptional divisor of this blow up and (by a slight abuse of notation)E‚äāZ 2 the proper transform ofEinZ 2
. Then, in classical language,Z=Bl őď (A 2 K ) is obtained fromZ 2 by blowing down E. In other words: Proposition IV-40.The blow-upZ  ofZ=Bl őď (A 2 K )at its singular point PisZ 2
.
E Z 1 L P L Z 2 E F L 0 Z L 0 F L a singular point ofZ A 2 L 0 L 
IV.2 Blow-ups181 We see from this description that the lines through the origin in the plane are not made disjoint, as they were in the case of the blow-up ofA 2 K at the reduced origin: they are made disjoint in the first blow-up, but then meet each other once more after we blow downE‚äāZ 2
. On the other hand, nonsingular curves through the origin tangent to thex-axis and having different curvatures are separated: after the first blow-up in this sequence they meet transversely at the pointP; they are then separated by the second blow-up and are not affected by the blowing down.
Proof of Proposition IV-40.By Exercise IV-35,  the blow-up ofZat its singular point is the same as the blow-up ofZat the reduced schemeF associated to the exceptional divisor ofZ=Bl őď (A 2 K )‚ÜíA 2 K
. This scheme Fis the total transform inZof the (reduced) origin inA 2 K , as we see directly from the equations.
On the other hand, we claim thatZ 2 may be obtained by first blowing up the reduced origin inA 2 K to getZ 1 , and then blowing up the total transform of őď inZ 1 ‚ÄĒ the reverse of the previous process. To see this, observe that by the equations the ideal of őď inZ 1 is the product of the ideal ofE‚äāZ 1 and the ideal of the pointP; sinceEis Cartier, it follows that Bl őď  Z 1 =Bl P Z 1
.
With these remarks in place, it now suffices to apply the following lemma: Lemma IV-41.LetXbe a scheme andY 1 andY 2 ‚äāXclosed subschemes.
Iff i :Z i =Bl Y i X‚ÜíXbe the blow-ups ofXalongY 1 andY 2 , then Bl f ‚ąí1 1 (Y 2 ) Z 1 ‚ąľ = Bl f ‚ąí1 2 (Y 1 ) Z 2 asX-schemes.
Proof.LetW 1 =Bl f ‚ąí1 1 (Y 2 ) Z 1 , and letg 1 :W 1 ‚ÜíZ 1 be  the blow-up map; defineW 2 andg 2 analogously. Seth i =f i ‚ó¶g i :W i ‚ÜíX.Since h ‚ąí1 1 (Y 2 )=g ‚ąí1 1 (f ‚ąí1 1 (Y 2 ))‚äāW 1 is Cartier, the structure maph 1 :W 1 ‚ÜíX factors throughZ 2 ; that is, there is a mapj 1 :W 1 ‚ÜíZ 2 such thath 1 = f 2 ‚ó¶j 1
. Similarly, sincej ‚ąí1 1 (f ‚ąí1 2 ((Y 1 )) =h ‚ąí1 1 (Y 1 )=g ‚ąí1 1 (f ‚ąí1 1 (Y 1 ))‚äāW 1 is also Cartier, the mapj 1 :W 1 ‚ÜíZ 2 factors throughW 2 =Bl f 2 ‚ąí1(Y 1 ) Z 2 , inducing a mapk 1 :W 1 ‚ÜíW 2 such thath 1 =h 2 ‚ó¶k 1
. In the other direction, we likewise obtain a mapk 2 :W 2 ‚ÜíW 1
. SinceW 1 has  no automorphisms as anX-scheme,k 2 ‚ó¶k 1 is the identity, and in particular k 1 is an isomorphism.
Compare this lemma with Exercises IV-37 to IV-39, where we saw that if we replace ‚Äútotal transform‚ÄĚ with ‚Äúproper transform‚ÄĚ, the order does indeed matter.
Blowing Up Multiple Points.We will consider here a few more exam- ples of blow-ups of the plane along subschemes supported at a point.
 182IV.  Classical Constructions Exercise IV-42.For another example, let ‚Ą¶ 1 ‚äāA 2 K be the subscheme defined by the ideal (y, x 3 )‚äāK[x, y] and ‚Ą¶ 2 the subscheme defined by the ideal (y 2 ,x 3 )‚äāK[x, y]. Consider the blow-upsŌÜ i :Z i =Bl ‚Ą¶ i (A 2 K )‚ÜíA 2 K of the plane at each of these two schemes. Show in that in each case the schemeZ i is singular, the fiberŌÜ ‚ąí1 i (P) over the originP=(x, y)‚ąąA 2 K is isomorphic toP 1 K
. Show also that in each case the blow-up map may be factored into a sequence of three blow-ups followed by two contractions, that is, there is a schemeW i , obtained by blowing upA 2 K successively at three reduced points, and a mapW i ‚ÜíZ i that is constant on the exceptional divisors of the first two blow-ups and is an isomorphism on their complement. What is different about the sequence of points blown up in the two cases?
Not to give a false impression, we should remark that the fibers of blow- ups, even of nonsingular varieties, need not be projective spaces. (Of course, given our assertion that any proper birational morphism is a blow-up, this could hardly be the case.) The subject of the following exercises is a simple example of other behavior.
Exercise IV-43.LetA 2 K =SpecK[x, y] be the affine plane over an alge- braically closed fieldK,and let őď‚äāA 2 K be the subscheme given by őď=V(x 3 ,xy,y 2 ).
LetXbe the blow-upX=Bl őď (A 2 K ). Show thatXis given as X= ProjK[x, y][A, B, C]/I whereIis the ideal I=(yA‚ąíx 2 B, yB‚ąíxC, AC‚ąíxB 2 ).
Hint:blow upA 3 K =Spec[x, y, z] along the subschemeV(z‚ąíxy, x 3 ,y 2 ), which is a regular subscheme, and consider the proper transform of the planeV(z).
Exercise IV-44.Show that the schemeXof the preceding exercise is nonsingular, with fiber over the origin (x, y)‚ąąA 2 K a union of two copies of P 1 K meeting at one point. (In fact,Xis the schemeZ 2 of Proposition IV-40.) It is not the case that we have a one-to-one correspondence between ideals and blow-ups; different ideals may yield the same blow-up. Of course there are many trivial examples of this ‚ÄĒ for example, any principal ideal yields the trivial blow-up. Only slightly less trivially, letXbe any Noetherian scheme, őď‚äāXany closed subscheme andI‚äāO X its ideal sheaf. Let őď n be the subscheme ofXdefined by the idealI n
. It follows from the definition via the universal property that the blow-upsZ n =Bl őď n (X) are all isomorphic. Here are some more interesting examples:

Thej-Function.Here is an example of a blow-up similar to the one we have just described that arises very naturally. It involves thej-function of a plane cubic curve; this is a topic we will not mention officially until the very end of this book, but with which the reader may well be familiar. In any event, we will assume some acquaintance withjin what follows.
We consider the (flat) familyE‚ÜíA 2 K =SpecK[a, b] of plane cubic curves given by the equation y 2 =x 3 +ax+b.
Now, when the curveC a,b , given inA 2 K =SpecK[x, y] by the equation y 2 =x 3 +ax+b, is nonsingular, we associate to it the scalar j(C a,b ) = 1728 4a 3 4a 3 +27b 2
.
As the reader may know, two such curvesC a,b andC a  ,b  are isomorphic if and only if the values of thej-function are the same. It is thus of some interest to understand how the rational map fromA 2 K =SpecK[a, b]to A 1 K =SpecK[j] behaves ‚ÄĒ in other words, how the moduli of the curve C a,b behaves when it becomes singular. Most of the time this is clear: if the point (a, b) approaches any point of the curve 4a 3 +27b 2 = 0 other than the originQ=(a, b)‚äāSpecK[a, b], the value ofj(C a,b ) approaches infinity. The question of what happens whenC a,b acquires a cusp is more subtle. To put it another way, we have a morphism j:A 2 K \{Q}‚ąí‚ÜíP 1 K (a, b)‚ąí‚Üíj(C a,b ) and would like to understand the map in a neighborhood ofQ.
The answer is not hard to find: the closure őď inA 2 K √óP 1 K of the graph of the mapj:A 2 K \{Q}‚ÜíP 1 K is simply the blow-up ŌÜ 2 :Z 2 =Bl ‚Ą¶ 2 (A 2 K )‚ÜíA 2 K of the plane along the subscheme whose ideal is generated by the numerator and denominator of the expression above forj(C a,b ). We can also describe it in terms of classical blow-ups as follows: 
184IV.  Classical Constructions Exercise IV-46.Factor the projection őď‚ÜíA 2 K into blow-ups and blow- downs at reduced points: explicitly, show that the mapjblows up the origin, then the point of intersection of the exceptional divisor with the proper transform of thex-axis, then the intersection of the two exceptional divisors; finally, it blows down the first two exceptional divisors.
From this description we can see many things. For example, consider a pencilof cubics specializing to a cusp; that is, restrict the family above to a line through the origin in the plane SpecK[a, b]. Equivalently, consider for some pairőĪ, ő≤the family of curvesC t given by y 2 =x 3 +őĪtx+ő≤t.
The limiting value ofj(C t )astapproaches 0 is alwaysj= 0 ‚ÄĒ in terms of the moduli spaceM 1 , the curves approach the curve given byy 2 =x 3 +1‚ÄĒ independently of the slopeő≤/őĪ, as long aső≤= 0. Conversely, if we want to describe families of plane cubics acquiring a cusp whosej-invariants approach a value other than 0 or‚ąě, we have to find curves through the origin in the plane SpecK[a, b] whose proper transform in the triple blow- upW 2 of the plane, described in Exercise IV-46, is separated from the first two exceptional divisors.
In this case thej-function is so explicitly given that we hardly need the geometric analysis. But the qualitative picture is very important: the picture in general when a family a curves of any genus acquires a cusp is the same. For example, if a pencil of plane curves acquires a cusp, the stable limit will always have an elliptic tail ofj-invariant either 0 or‚ąě.
IV.2.4   Blow-ups of Arithmetic Schemes Since we have defined blow-ups so generally, we can use the construction to relate various arithmetic schemes, as the following examples and exercises illustrate.
We start by blowing up a reduced point inP 1 Z : we letPbe the reduced pointP=(3,X)‚ąąP 1 Z and consider the blow-upZ=Bl P (P 1 Z )ofP 1 Z at P.This is straightforward; as before, the only problem is notational. Example IV-54.Consider the schemes C 1 = ProjZ[X, Y, Z]/(XY‚ąí3Z 2 ), C 3 = ProjZ[X, Y, Z]/(XY‚ąí27Z 2 ), C 2 = ProjZ[X, Y, Z]/(XY‚ąí9Z 2 ), C 4 = ProjZ[X, Y, Z]/(XY‚ąí81Z 2 ).

Moreover, the inverse images of the open subset S=SpecZ[ 1 3 ] = SpecZ\{(3)}‚äāSpecZ in all four are isomorphic, via (powers of) the automorphism of the ring Z[ 1 3 ,X,Y,Z] given by (X, Y, Z)‚Üí(3X, Y, Z). .
We claim, however, thatno two of these schemes are isomorphic; and we will prove this as an illustration of the various techniques developed over the course of this section. The key is the local structure of each scheme around the point (3,X,Y) (which we will, by a slight abuse of notation, callPin each of the four schemesC i ). We start as follows: Exercise IV-55.Show thatC 1 is nonsingular, whileC 2 ,C 3 andC 4 each havePas a unique singular point.
Thus,C 1 cannot be isomorphic to any of the others; and for any two of the others to be isomorphic, a neighborhood ofPin each must be isomorphic.
Now, we cannot use the dimension of the tangent space toC i atPto further distinguish among these:T P (C 1 ) is two-dimensional (sinceC 1 is nonsingular, after all), and dimT P (C i ) = 3 for each ofi=2,3 and 4. But the tangent cone does provide a useful tool here: Exercise IV-56.Show that the projective tangent cone toC 2 atPis a nonsingular plane conic, while the tangent cones toC 3 andC 4 atPare each a union of two distinct lines inP 2 Z/(3)
.
ThusC 2 cannot be isomorphic to any of the others. Finally, how do we distinguishC 3 andC 4 ? Blowing up provides the answer: Exercise IV-57.Let  ŐÉ C 3 =Bl P (C 3 ) be the blow-up ofC 3 atP,and  ŐÉ C 4 the blow-up ofC 4 atP.Show that  ŐÉ C 3 is nonsingular, while  ŐÉ C 4 is not.

In fact, the above analysis shows something more than is claimed: we see that the local ringsO C n ,p (equivalently, the local schemes SpecO C n ,p ) are not isomorphic to one another pairwise.
IV.2.5   Project: Quadric and Cubic Surfaces as Blow-ups It is a classical fact that a nonsingular quadric surfaceQ‚äāP 3 C is isomorphic to the surface obtained by blowing up two points in the planeP 2 C and blowing down the line joining them ‚ÄĒ in other words, the blow-up ofQat a point is isomorphic to the blow-up ofP 2 C at two points. (This description arises naturally if we consider the graph őď of the rational mapQ‚ÜíP 2 C given by projection from a point onQ.) It is likewise well-known, if less readily seen, that a nonsingular cubic surfaceS‚äāP 2 C is isomorphic to the blow-up of the plane at six points, no three collinear and not all six on a conic.
In the following series of exercises, we will see how to use our notion of blow-ups along arbitrary subschemes of the plane to extend this description of smooth quadric and cubic surfaces to some singular ones. We start with the case of quadric surfaces. Here we ask: what do we get if, instead of blowing up two points and blowing down the line joining them, we blow up a nonreduced scheme őď‚äāP 2 K of degree 2 and dimension 0, and blow down the unique line containing it? The answer is expressed in the following: Exercise IV-59.LetKbe an algebraically closed field,Q‚äāP 3 K an irre- ducible quadric, andp‚ąąQany nonsingular closed point. Show that the blow-up ofQatpis isomorphic to the blow-up of the planeP 2 K at a sub- scheme őď‚äāP 2 K of dimension zero and degree 2, with őď reduced if and only ifQis nonsingular.
The situation over non-algebraically closed fields is illustrated in the following two exercises.

The surfaceS‚äāP 2 K √ó K P 3 K projects isomorphically toP 3 K , and its image is a smooth cubic surface; conversely, every smooth cubicS‚äāP 3 K may be obtained in this way.

IV.3   Fano schemes IV.3.1   Definitions In classical geometry, one way to study a projective varietyX‚äāP n K is via its relation to linear subspaces ofP n K
. Thus, a number of subvarieties of the GrassmanniansG K (k, n) are associated to such a variety. For example, we can associate toX‚äāP n K the loci inG K (k, n) of linear spaces that meetX; of tangent spaces toX; of secants toX; or of linear spaces contained inX.
All of these subvarieties can now be redefined as subschemes ofG S (k, n) associated to a subschemeX‚äāP n S , and as such they are endowed with a richer structure that reflects the geometry ofX.Even if we start with a varietyX‚äāP n K over an algebraically closed fieldK,the schemes associated to it in this way may be nonreduced.
In this section we will define and study the schemeF k (X)‚äāG S (k, n) parametrizing linear spaces of dimensionkcontained in a schemeX‚äā P n S ; this is called thek-thFano schemeofX.We will try in particular to indicate how and when a nonreduced scheme structure may arise, and how it allows us to extend many classical theorems about Fano varieties.
For example, we‚Äôll see that, ifKis any field andX‚äāP 3 K is any cubic surface not swept out by lines, the Fano scheme of lines onXwill have degree exactly 27 overK,though the set of lines contained inXwill have cardinality 27 only ifXis nonsingular, and even then may not ifKis not algebraically closed. More generally, we will see that in many cases the family of Fano schemes associated to a flat family of varietiesX‚äāP n B ‚ÜíB is itself flat overB,and so we will be able to make statements about number and degree in greater generality.
In this chapter we will define Fano schemes by giving their defining ideals, which are the same ideals that were classically used to define the Fano variety; the only difference is that we no longer throw away information by passing to their radicals. However, we will see in Chapter VI that there is a more intrinsic definition of Fano schemesF k (X) using the functor of points; 
IV.3 Fano schemes193 and this definition gives us in turn a characterization of various aspects of their geometry (e.g., their tangent spaces) that is more directly related to the geometry of the schemesX.These descriptions are very useful even in case bothXandF k (X) are varieties.
LetSbe any scheme, and letX‚äāP n S be any subscheme of projective space overS; letk<nbe any positive integer. The Fano schemeF k (X)‚äā G=G S (k, n)ofXis a scheme parametrizing the  linear subspaces  of dimensionkinP n S lying onX.(As always, the word ‚Äúparametrize‚ÄĚ has a precise meaning, which we will discuss further in Section VI.2.2 below.) We define theF k (X) first in case the base schemeS=SpecRis affine. We will describe them in terms of the description given in Section III.2.7 ofGas the union of affine spacesW I ‚ąľ = A (k+1)(n‚ąík) S
.
Recall that in this construction we let W=SpecR[...,x i,j ,...] ‚ąľ = A (k+1)(n+1) S (which we think of as the affine space associated to the vector space of (k+1)√ó(n+ 1) matrices), and for each multi-indexI=(i 0 ,...,i k )‚äā {0,1,...,n}letW I ‚ąľ = A (k+1)(n‚ąík) S ‚äāWbe the closed subscheme given by the ideal (...,x i őĪ ,j ő≤ ‚ąíőī őĪ,ő≤ ,...) (which we think of as the affine space asso- ciated to the subspace of matrixes whoseI-th submatrix is the identity).
Now, suppose thatG(Z 0 ,...,Z n )‚ąąI(X) is any homogeneous polynomial in the ideal ofX.Applying it to a general linear combination of the rows of a (k+1)√ó(n+ 1) matrix, we obtain a polynomial H G (u, x)=G !
 u i x 0,i ,  u i x 1,i ,...,  u i x k,i " which we may write out as a linear combination of the monomialsu J = u j 0 0 u j 1 1 ¬∑¬∑¬∑u j k k in the variablesu 0 ,...,u k : H G (u, x)=  H G,J (x)¬∑u J
.
The coefficient polynomialsH G,J (x) are then polynomials in the variables x i,j ; restricting to the subschemeW I ‚ąľ = A (k+1)(n‚ąík) S ‚äāWthey are like- wise regular functions there. We define theFano schemeF k (X)tobethe subscheme ofGgiven, in each open subsetW I , by the ideal generated by the polynomialsH G,J (x), whereGranges over all elements of the ideal I(X)‚äāR[Z 0 ,...,Z n ] andJindexes monomials of degreedin the variables u 0 ,...,u k
.
Alternatively, for any (k+ 1)-tuplec=(c 0 ,...,c k ) of elements ofR,we may define a polynomialH G,c (x)by H G,c (x)=G !

 194IV.  Classical Constructions To complete this definition we would have to check a number of things: that these subschemes ofW I agree on the overlaps of theW I , and that the subschemeF k (X)‚äāGthey define does not depend on choice of coordinates (this is easier if we adopt the second way of generating the ideal ofF k (X)‚ą© W I , but then of course we have to show the two ways yield the same ideal).
Finally, we should check that the construction is natural, that is, ifT‚ÜíS is any morphism andX T =X√ó S T‚äāP n T , then the Fano schemeF k (X T )= F k (X)√ó S T‚äāG S (k, n)√ó S T=G T (k, n). This last condition in particular ensures that, given a projective schemeX‚äāP n S over an arbitrary (possibly nonaffine) baseS,we can define the Fano schemeF k (X)‚äāG S (k, n)by restricting to affine open subschemes ofSand gluing the results. All of these assertions can either be verified directly from the definitions; but they will follow more readily from the intrinsic characterization of the Grassmannian and of Fano schemes to be given in Section VI.2.2 below.
IV.3.2   Lines on Quadrics To illustrate the definition of Fano schemes, we will consider a simple case: the lines on the quadric surfaceQ=V(X 2 +Y 2 +Z 2 +W 2 )‚äāP 3 K over an algebraically closed fieldK.For convenience, we assume the characteristic ofKis not 2 (the situation is the same in characteristic 2 as long as we stick to smooth quadrics). Even in this case, we will see some very interesting phenomena; and we will consider some examples over non-algebraically closed fields as well.
Lines on a Smooth Quadric over an Algebraically Closed Field.
As suggested above, we will first write down equations forF 1 (Q)inan open subsetW I ‚äāG=G K (1,3); in this case, symmetry will do the rest.

This is a nice example of a scheme arising in a purely geometric context that is reducible but not absolutely irreducible.
A Quadric Degenerating to Two Planes.Consider now a family of quadricsQ‚ÜíBwhose general member is smooth, specializing to a quadric Q 0 consisting of the union of two planes. What is fascinating about this case is not the Fano schemeF 1 (Q 0 ) ‚ÄĒ after all, sinceQ 0 is a union of two planes, the locus of lines lying on it is pretty simple ‚ÄĒ but the geometry of the family. In our main example the Fano schemeF 1 (Q)‚äāG B (1,3) is not flat overB(and indeed the restriction of the family to the open subset 
IV.3 Fano schemes199 B ‚ąó =B\{0}has no flat limit at 0); in other examples a flat limit exists but depends on the particular family and not just onQ 0
.
All this is easier to do than to say. To begin with, letB=SpecK[s, t] ‚ąľ = A 2 K , and consider the familyQ‚äāP 3 B ‚ÜíBgiven by Q=V(sX 2 +tY 2 +Z 2 +W 2 )‚äāProjK[s, t][X, Y, Z, W] and letQ őľ,őĹ ‚äāP 3 K be the fiber ofQover the point (s‚ąíőľ, t‚ąíőĹ)‚ąąB.Let F 1 (Q)‚äāG B (1,3) be the Fano scheme ofQ.
Even without writing down equations, we can see thatF 1 (Q) is not flat overB: the fibersF 1 (Q őľ,őĹ )ofF 1 (Q)overBare all one-dimensional, except for the one fiberF 1 (Q 0,0 ), which is visibly two-dimensional, having support the union of two planes. To see more, we write the equations. As before, we start with the equations ofF 1 (Q) in the open subsetW X,Y ofG B (1,3); we have F 1 (Q)‚ą©W X,Y =V(t+a 2 +b 2 ,ac+bd, s+c 2 +d 2 )‚äāSpecK[s, t][a, b, c, d].
The fiber ofF 1 (Q) over the origin (s, t)‚ąąBis given inW X,Y by F 1 (Q 0,0 )‚ą©W X,Y =V(a 2 +b 2 ,ac+bd, c 2 +d 2 )‚äāA 4 K
.
We may also describe this as the union of the two planes őď 1 and őď 2 ‚äāA 4 K given by őď 1 =V(a+ ‚ąö ‚ąí1b, c‚ąí ‚ąö ‚ąí1d), őď 2 =V(a‚ąí ‚ąö ‚ąí1b, c+ ‚ąö ‚ąí1d).

Let us consider now subfamilies of this two-parameter family. To start with, let us fix two nonzero scalarsőĪandő≤‚ąąK,and consider the restric- tion of our family to the lineV(ő≤s‚ąíőĪt) through the origin inB=A 2 K with slopeő≤/őĪ; that is, the familyQ őĪ,ő≤ with baseB  =SpecK[u] given by Q őĪ,ő≤ =V(őĪuX 2 +ő≤uY 2 +Z 2 +W 2 )‚äāProjK[u][X, Y, Z, W] Again, the Fano schemeF 1 (Q őĪ,ő≤ ) is not flat overB  , for the same reason.
What is different here is that the Fano scheme over the complement of the origin inB  doeshave a flat limit. In fact,F 1 (Q őĪ,ő≤ ) is reducible, with F 1 (Q 0,0 ) one component; and if we remove that component what is left is flat. To see this, we write the equations ofF 1 (Q őĪ,ő≤ ) in the open subset W X,Y ofG B (1,3): F 1 (Q őĪ,ő≤ )‚ą©W X,Y =V(őĪu+a 2 +b 2 ,ac+bd, ő≤u+c 2 +d 2 ) ‚äāSpecK[őĪ, ő≤][a, b, c, d].
Let ő¶(ő≥)‚äāA 4 K be the 2-plane given by ő¶(ő≥)=V(a‚ąíő≥d, b+ő≥c) 
200IV.  Classical Constructions and let ő®(ő≥) be the disjoint union of ő¶(ő≥) and ő¶(‚ąíő≥). What we see from these equations is that, for everyőľ=0‚ąąK,the fiber ofF 1 (Q őĪ,ő≤ )over (u‚ąíőľ)‚ąąB  is contained in the scheme ő®( , őĪ/ő≤),independently ofőľ, and is cut out on ő®( , őĪ/ő≤) by the one further equationő≤u+c 2 +d 2 =0.It follows that the flat limit of the Fano schemesF 1 (Q őĪu,ő≤u )asuapproaches 0 is the intersection of ő®( , őĪ/ő≤) with the unionV(c 2 +d 2 ) of the two hyperplanesV(c+ ‚ąö ‚ąí1d) andV(c‚ąí ‚ąö ‚ąí1d). This is the union of four lines.
To interpret this geometrically, note that the first of these lines is simply the subscheme of the GrassmannianG K (1,3) of lines lying in the plane H 1 =V(Z+ ‚ąö ‚ąí1W) and passing through the point P 1 =[ ‚ąö ‚ąí1 , ő≤,‚ąí ‚ąö őĪ,0,0]; the second is the subscheme of lines lying in the planeH 1 and passing through the pointP 2 =[ ‚ąö ‚ąí1 ‚ąö ő≤, ‚ąö őĪ,0,0]; the third the subscheme of lines lying in the planeH 2 =V(Z‚ąí ‚ąö ‚ąí1W) and passing through the pointP 1 , and the last the subscheme of lines lying in the planeH 2 and passing through the pointP 2
. Note that the pointsP 1 andP 2 here may be characterized as the intersection of the double lineM=H 1 ‚ą©H 2 of the quadricQ 0 with the other quadricsQ őĪőľ,ő≤őľ in the pencil.
The flat limits of the familiesQ őĪ,ő≤ vary as the ratioő≤/őĪvaries. In par- ticular, their union is dense inF 1 (Q 0,0 ). This shows that the Fano scheme F 1 (Q)‚ÜíBof the whole family is irreducible, and hence that the restric- tion ofF 1 (Q) to the complement of the origin (s, t)‚ąąBdoes not have a flat limit.

IV.3.3   Lines on Cubic Surfaces In the following series of exercises, we will develop some interesting facts about the Fano schemeF 1 (S) parametrizing the lines on a cubic surfaceS‚äā P 3 K
. To begin with, the following two exercises establish that all nonsingular cubics contain the same number of lines (without deriving the number 27).
 202IV.  Classical Constructions Exercise IV-73.LetKbe a field,S‚äāP 3 K a nonsingular cubic surface, andF=F 1 (S)‚äāG K (1,3) the Fano scheme of lines onS.Show thatFis reduced.
Hint: TakeL‚ąąF 1 (S) the line given byX=Y= 0, and write the cubic polynomial definingSasXQ(Z, W)+YP(Z, W) modulo (X, Y) 2 ; show that the condition that the tangent spaceT [L] F 1 (S) be positive-dimensional is thatPandQhave a common zero alongL. (Compare this to the dis- cussion in Section VI.2.3.) Exercise IV-74.LetP 19 K be the projective space parametrizing cubic sur- faces inP 3 K , andU‚äāP 19 K the open subset corresponding to cubics hav- ing only finitely many lines (that is, other than cones and scrolls). Let S U ‚äāP 3 U be the universal cubic in projective 3-space overU,andF U = F 1 (S U )‚äāG U (1,3) be its Fano scheme of lines. Show that the projection mapŌÄ:F U ‚ÜíUis flat.
Hint:Use the fact that the Fano schemeF 1 (S) of lines on a cubic surface S‚äāP 3 K is a local complete intersection, together with Proposition II-32.
In fact, neither of the statements of the two preceding exercises is com- pletely general: that is, for somedandnthe Fano varietyF 1 (X) of lines on a hypersurfaceX‚äāP n K of degreedmay be singular and even nonreduced forXnonsingular; and the dimension dimF 1 (X) may jump asXvaries, so that the universal Fano schemeFneed not be flat. We will be able to exhibit examples of these behaviors once we have developed a description of tangent spaces to Fano schemes in Chapter VI.
We now consider what happens when our cubic surface becomes singular.
There are essentially two cases to consider. In some sense the simpler case is whenShas only isolated double points: in this case (as we will see) the Fano schemeF 1 (S) is still 0-dimensional of degree 27, and fits into a flat family with the Fano schemes of nonsingular cubics (that is, in terms of Exercise IV-74 above, if we letU  ‚äāP 19 K be the larger open subset of cubics having at most isolated double points as singularities, and define S U  ‚äāP 3 U  andF U  ‚äāG U  (1,3) accordingly, thenF U  ‚ÜíU  is still flat).
On the other hand, whenSis a cone or has positive-dimensional singular locus ‚ÄĒ for example, whenSis reducible ‚ÄĒF 1 (S) will become positive- dimensional; and a further question arises: what may be the flat limit of the Fano schemesF 1 (S őĽ )‚äāG(1,3) asS őĽ approachesS?
We start with some examples of cubic surfaces having isolated double points. For this it may be useful to recall the description of such surfaces in Exercise IV-62 above, or in Griffiths and Harris [1978].

IV.4   Forms LetSbe any scheme andXany scheme overS.We say that a schemeYover Sis aformofXif for every pointp‚ąąSthere exists an open neighborhood UofpinSand a flat surjective morphismT‚ÜíUof schemes such that Y√ó S T ‚ąľ = X√ó S TasT-schemes.
To begin with a classic example, consider the conic in the real projective planeP 2 R given by the equationX 2 +Y 2 +Z 2 = 0, that is, the curve C= ProjR[X, Y, Z]/(X 2 +Y 2 +Z 2 ). The curveChas no points defined over R‚ÄĒthat is, no points with residue fieldR‚ÄĒand so cannot be isomorphic to the projective lineP 1 R
. However, the result of extending the ground field to the algebraic closureCofRisC√ó SpecR SpecC ‚ąľ = ProjC[X, Y, Z]/(X 2 + Y 2 +Z 2 ) ‚ąľ = P 1 C
.ThusCis a form ofP 1 R over SpecR, or, more succinctly, anR-form of the projective line.
As a second example, the reader might check that the field extension SpecQ[x]/(x 2 +1) over SpecQis a form of the scheme consisting of two distinct points, while SpecZ/(2)[x]/(x 2 +1) over SpecZ/(2) is a form of a double point.
In number theory it is of interest to see how the set of rational points may vary in a family of forms of a given curve. To give a particular case, for anyt‚ąąQthe set of rational solutions (x, y) of what is calledPell‚Äôs equation, ty 2 =x 2 ‚ąí1, is the set ofQ-rational points on the curveC t =SpecQ[x, y]/(ty 2 ‚ąíx 2 + 1).
These curvesC t are forms ofP 1 over SpecQ.Likewise, the curvesE t = SpecQ[x, y]/(ty 2 ‚ąíx 3 + 1) all havej-invariant 0, and thus are all forms of the curveE 1 ‚äāP 2 Q given byy 2 =x 3 ‚ąí1 (see Section IV.2.3 above and Section VI.2.4 below), but have varying arithmetic properties.
In each of these cases, it is easy to see that the curves given are forms of each other‚ÄĒthe curvesE t √ó SpecQ SpecQ[ ‚ąö t] andE 1 √ó SpecQ SpecQ[ ‚ąö t] are visibly isomorphic‚ÄĒand less obvious but not hard to see that they are not all isomorphic. (In fact the naive guess‚ÄĒthatE t ‚ąľ = E 1 if and only if 
IV.4 Forms205 t‚ąą(Q ‚ąó ) 2 , that is,tis the square of a nonzero rational number‚ÄĒis correct, but it is a nontrivial exercise to prove this.) The set of isomorphism classes ofS-forms of projective spaceP n , for all n, is in a natural way a group, called theBrauer groupofS(or, in case S=SpecKis the spectrum of a field, the Brauer group ofK), with the true projective spaceP n S the identity element. This group may be computed with Galois cohomology; see Serre [1975]. Here is a construction of the Brauer group related to number theory: LetKbe a field, and letAbe ann-dimensionalAzumaya algebraover K‚ÄĒthat is,Ais an algebra which has dimensionnas a vector space, has no nontrivial 2-sided ideals, and has center exactlyK. For example, the algebraM d (K) consisting of alld√ódmatrices overKis ad 2 -dimensional Azumaya algebra. It follows from the Wedderburn structure theorems that ifAis ann-dimensional Azumaya algebra overKthennis a square, say n=d 2 , andK‚äóA ‚ąľ = M d (K) ‚ąľ = K‚äóM d (K) (in this senseAis a form of M d (K).) IdentifyingM d (K) with the endomorphism algebra of ad-dimensional vector spaceVoverK, it is easy to see that the left ideals ofM d (K) each have the form {a‚ąąM d (K)|Im(a)‚äāW} for some subspaceW‚äāV.The vector space dimension of the left ideal is then dim(V) dim(W). In particular, the left ideals of dimension equal to d(d‚ąí1) correspond to the hyperplanes inV,that is, the points ofP(V).
The subscheme of the Grassmannian ofd(d‚ąí1)-planes inM d (K) that are closed under multiplication byM d (K) ‚ÄĒ that is, are ideals ‚ÄĒ is isomorphic toP d (K) in this way.
At the other extreme, there are (in general) Azumaya algebrasAthat are division algebras overK‚ÄĒ that is, algebras with no left ideals at all. But we can still form thescheme of left idealsofA. Of course we must specify it by equations, not as a point set! LetGbe the Grassmannian ofd(d‚ąí1)- dimensional subspaces of the vector spaceA.IfS‚äāA G is the universal bundle then the subscheme ofGthat we want is the largest subschemeX such that the restriction toXof the composite map of vector bundles S‚äóA G ‚äāA G ‚äóA G multiplication  A  A/S onGis zero (this is locally a subscheme defined by thed(d‚ąí1)d 2 √ódentries in a matrix representing the composite map.) Extending the ground field, we find thatX K is the Grassmannian ofd(d‚ąí1) dimensional ideals ofK‚äóA, that is, projective space! Conversely, it can be shown that every form of projective space overKoccurs in this way and that the correspondence between isomorphism classes of forms and Azumaya algebras is one-to-one.
For all this see Serre [1975] and Cassels and Fr Őąohlich [1967].

 V Local Constructions V.1   Images In this section we will be concerned with a basic notion: theimageof a morphism in the category of schemes. As we will see, there are two fun- damental properties that we would like the notion of image to have, the push-pull propertyandinvariance under base change; but the two are in- compatible. We will give accordingly two definitions, one straightforward and one less so, each of which is useful in certain situations.
V.1.1   The Image of a Morphism of Schemes SupposeŌÜ:X‚ÜíYis a morphism of schemes. Theset-theoretic imageof ŌÜis defined in the obvious way: it is the subset ofYconsisting of those pointsy‚ąąYsuch that there is a pointx‚ąąXwithŌÜ(x)=y. The image may or may not be a closed subset: for example, ifXis the scheme defined by the ideal (xy‚ąí1)‚äāK[x, y]inA 2 K =SpecK[x, y], then the image of the projection ofXto the affine lineA 1 K =SpecK[x] is the complement of the origin, an open set. In a certain sense this is because we ‚Äúforgot‚ÄĚ some of the points of the source scheme: if we extend this morphism to a morphism from the closure ofXinA 1 K √ó K P 1 K , the image becomes the whole projective line.
These two examples turn out to be typical. The situation is summed up in the following theorem. To state it, recall that a subsetVof a topological space isconstructibleif it is a finite union of locally closed subsetsV i
.
 210V.  Local Constructions Theorem V-1.If the morphism of Noetherian schemesŌÜ:X‚ÜíYis of finite type, the set-theoretic image ofŌÜis constructible inY.If the morphismŌÜis projective, the set-theoretic image ofŌÜis closed.
The first of these statements is due to Chevalley. Note the necessity of the hypothesis thatŌÜis of finite type: the morphism SpecK[x 1 ,...,x n ] (x 1 ,...,x n ) ‚ąí‚ÜíSpecK[x 1 ,...,x n ], coming from the inclusion of the polynomial ring in its localization, does not have constructible image for anyn. The second statement, which is quite old, is generally called the Main Theorem of Elimination Theory. The definition of properness, which is a strengthening of the conclusion of the theorem, was essentially made to express this property (see Section III.1).
The proof of Theorem V-1 is exactly the same as in the classical case of varieties; see for example Harris [1995] or Hartshorne [1977]. We will not repeat it here.
More novel is the fact that the closure of the image has a natural scheme structure, and this is the fact that we shall explore in this section.
Suppose now we wish to define the image of a morphismŌÜ:X‚ÜíY as a scheme. What we would like ideally ‚ÄĒ though, as we shall see, this is impossible ‚ÄĒ is to takeŌÜ(X) to be the smallest subscheme ofYwhose inverse image is all ofX.In other words, we would characterizeŌÜ(X)by thepush-pull property: for every subschemeZ‚äāY, Z‚äÉŌÜ(X)‚áź‚áíŌÜ ‚ąí1 (Z)=X.
As it happens, though, no such subscheme ofYneed exist, as shown by the example of the morphism ŌÜ:X=A 2 K ‚ąí‚ÜíY=A 2 K defined by the ring homomorphismŌÜ # :K[x, y]‚ÜíK[s, t] takingxtos andytost.
 V.1 Images211 For anyőĽ‚ąąK, letY őĽ be the complement inYof the point (0,őĽ). For őĽ= 0, the inverse image ofY őĽ is all ofX. Thus, if there were a subscheme ŌÜ(X)‚äāYsatisfying the push-pull property above withZ=Y, the support ofŌÜ(X) would be contained in the setI=  őĽ=0 Y őĽ , which is the union of the point (0,0) and the complement of thex-axis. On the other hand, from ŌÜ ‚ąí1 ŌÜ(X)=Xit would follow that the support ofŌÜ(X) containedI, and hence that suppŌÜ(X)=I. ButIis not a locally closed subset ofY, and so is not the support of any subscheme ofY.
We are, however, close to what we want: there does exist a smallestclosed subscheme ofYwhose inverse image is all ofX,and this subscheme does satisfy the push-pull property with respect to closed subschemesZ‚äāY.
Definition V-2.IfŌÜ:X‚ÜíYis a morphism of finite type, then the scheme-theoretic image, written  ŐĄŌÜ(X), is the closed subscheme ofYwhose sheaf of ideals is the sheaf of regular functions on open subsets ofYthat pull back to 0 underŌÜ # , that is,  ŐĄŌÜ(X)=V  Ker(ŌÜ # :O Y ‚ÜíŌÜ ‚ąó O X )  ‚äāY.
We say thatŌÜisdominantif   ŐĄŌÜ(X)=Y,or equivalently if the pullback mapŌÜ # is a monomorphism.
This condition that a morphism be dominant isnotjust a condition on the underlying map of topological spaces: for example, the inclusion ŌÜ:SpecK‚ÜíSpecK[]/( 2 ) is a surjection on underlying sets, but the image is a proper closed subscheme, the pullback mapŌÜ # is not injective, and the map is not dominant.
Proposition V-3.IfŌÜ:X‚ÜíYis a morphism of schemes, the closure of the set-theoretic image is ŐĄŌÜ(x) red
.
Proof.We may reduce at once to the affine case, and prove that ifŌÜ # : B‚ÜíAis a ring homomorphism then the intersectionJof all the primesQ ofBthat may be written in the formQ=(ŌÜ # ) ‚ąí1 (P) for some primePof Ais the radical of Ker(ŌÜ # ) (see Section I.2.1). LetI‚äāAbe the nilradical ofA.Wehave J= 	 P‚äāAprime (ŌÜ # ) ‚ąí1 (P)=(ŌÜ # ) ‚ąí1 (I).
Thus iff‚ąąJthenŌÜ # (f)‚ąąIis nilpotent, andf‚ąąrad(KerŌÜ # ). The opposite inequality is immediate.
It is sometimes convenient to work in an apparently more general case: IfŌÜ:X‚ÜíYis any morphism andX  ‚äāXa closed subscheme, then we define thescheme-theoretic image ŐĄŌÜ(X  )ofX  to be the subscheme defined by the sheaf of idealsIwith I(U)={f‚ąąO Y (U)|ŌÜ # (f)‚ąąI X  (ŌÜ ‚ąí1 (U)}.
 212V.  Local Constructions This ‚Äúgeneralization‚ÄĚ actually describes a special case, since   ŐĄŌÜ(X  ) is the scheme-theoretic image of the composite morphismX  ‚ÜíX‚ÜíY.
If the two schemesXandYare affine and the mapŌÜis dominant, then the ideal describing the image of a subschemeX  ‚äāXis obtained in a particularly simple way: we may think ofA(Y) as a subring ofA(X), and  ŐĄŌÜ(X  ) is the subscheme ofYdefined byI X  ‚ą©A(Y). V.1.2   Universal Formulas Is there a ‚Äúformula‚ÄĚ for the closure of the image of a map, and if so, what is it? This question, in a somewhat different language, occupied a large number of mathematicians in the past, and the theory is correspondingly rich. In many special cases beautiful and useful formulas were found for the set-theoretic image; the equations they give are usually referred to as resultants, a notion we will discuss below. The scheme-theoretic image is significantly more complicated: with a reasonable interpretation of the basic question there can be no formula for the scheme theoretic image! We shall next explain this fact and the opportunities to which it gives rise. We shall keep the discussion informal, but it can be formalized using the notion of family of schemes given in Section II.3.4.
Consider a consequence that a universal formula would have: it would be preserved by base change, or, put more informally, it would specialize on substitution of variables. Some examples will make this clear.
Example V-6.(See figure on the next page.) To begin with, letKbe an algebraically closed field, setB=SpecK[t]=A 1 K , and consider the projection map ŌÜ:A 2 B =SpecK[t, x, y]‚ÜíA 1 B =SpecK[t, x] defined by the inclusionK[t, x]‚äāK[t, x, y]. We regard this map infor- mally as a (trivial) family of projection mapsA 2 K =SpecK[x, y]‚ÜíA 1 K = SpecK[x] parametrized byt‚ąąK.Consider first the closed subscheme X‚äāA 2 B =A 3 K given as the union of the two disjoint linesV(y, x) and V(y‚ąí1,x+t); that is, X=V(y 2 ‚ąíy, yx+yt, yx‚ąíx, x 2 +tx).
We will think ofXas a family of pairs of points in the (x, y)-plane, param- etrized byt, and each with its projection onto thex-axis: for each scalar a‚ąąKwe letX a ‚äāA 2 K be the fiber ofXover the point (t‚ąía)‚ąąB, Y a ‚ąľ = A 1 K the fiber ofA 1 B over (t‚ąía) andŌÜ a :X a ‚ÜíY a the restriction of ŌÜtoX a
.
For each scalar valuea=0‚ąąKthe set-theoretic imageŌÜ(X a ) is the union of the two pointsx= 0 andx=‚ąía; fora= 0 it is simply the origin x= 0. The scheme-theoretic image   ŐĄŌÜ(X a ) is the subscheme of SpecK[x] defined by the idealI a :=K[x]‚ą©(y 2 ‚ąíy, yx+ay, yx‚ąíx, x 2 +ax). Since K[x, y]/(y 2 ‚ąíy, yx+ay, yx‚ąíx, x 2 +ax)=K[x, y]/(y 2 ‚ąíy, x+ay), 
214V.  Local Constructions X a X 0  ŐĄŌÜ(X a )=  ŐĄŌÜ(X) a  ŐĄŌÜ(X 0 )= ŐĄŌÜ(X) 0 we see that I a = Ker  K[x]‚ÜíK[x, y]/(y 2 ‚ąíy, x+ay)  =  (x 2 +ax)ifa=0, (x)ifa=0.
Thus the scheme-theoretic image  ŐĄŌÜ(X a ) is the union of two reduced points fora= 0 and a single reduced point fora=0.
The important thing about this example is that the scheme-theoretic images   ŐĄŌÜ(X a ) do not fit intoanyfamily of schemes! That is, there is no polynomialf(t, x) ‚Äúgiving a formula for the scheme-theoretic image‚ÄĚ in the sense that for eacha‚ąąKthe scheme-theoretic image   ŐĄŌÜ(X a ) is defined by the idealf(a, x) = 0. Indeed, fora= 0 the scheme   ŐĄŌÜ(X a ) is defined by the ideal (x 2 +ax), so we would have to havef(t, x)=g(t, x)(x 2 +tx) for some polynomialg(t, x). Sinceg(a, x)= 0 for allxwhena= 0, we must haveg(t, x)=g(t), a polynomial of one variable vanishing at most when t=0.Ifnowg(0) = 0 thenf(0,a) would describe the whole line, while if g(0)= 0 thenf(0,a) would describe a double point, and neither of these options is the scheme-theoretic image  ŐĄŌÜ(X a ).
Perhaps the best we can do in this example is to take the scheme-theoretic image of the whole family,   ŐĄŌÜ(X)‚äāSpecK[x, t]. This image is defined by the idealK[t, x]‚ą©(y 2 ‚ąíy, yx+yt, yx‚ąíx, x 2 +tx). To compute this inter- section, one shows that the localization map K[t, x, y]/(y 2 ‚ąíy, yx+yt, yx‚ąíx, x 2 +tx) ‚ÜíK[t, t ‚ąí1 ,x,y]/(y 2 ‚ąíy, yx+yt, yx‚ąíx, x 2 +tx) =K[t, t ‚ąí1 ,x]/(x 2 +tx) is a monomorphism, and it follows at once that the intersection is (x 2 +tx).
 V.1 Images215 We thus see that the scheme-theoretic image   ŐĄŌÜ(X 0 ) of the fiber ofX over the origin (t)‚ąąBis properly contained in the fiber   ŐĄŌÜ(X) 0 of the scheme-theoretic image  ŐĄŌÜ(X) over the origin. This choice of ‚Äúapproximation‚ÄĚ for a defining equation of scheme-theoretic images is a resultant, in a sense that we shall describe.

Note that, in the previous case,Y  =Y b was the fiber of a morphism Y‚ÜíB,but we need not assume that in general.
Proof.We reduce at once to the affine case, and consider this diagram of coordinate rings: A  =A‚äó B B   Ōą # A B  ŌÜ #   Ōą # B ŌÜ #  In this setting the first assertion becomes the inequality Ker(ŌÜ # )‚äÉŌą # (Ker(ŌÜ # ))B  , which is immediate from the commutativity of the diagram.

The closedness of the set-theoretic image ofŌÜwhenŌÜis finite follows if we takeY  to be a point ofYin the closure of the set-theoretic image (and thus in the scheme- theoretic image) and pull back via the inclusion morphism of this point.

Despite the nonexistence of a universal formula for the scheme-theoretic image, there are, as we have mentioned, many formulas giving equations that define it set-theoretically, and each of them gives a scheme containing the scheme-theoretic image, as one sees from the previous proposition. By Proposition V-8, these equations define a scheme inYcontaining the scheme-theoretic image, and in some cases, such as whenŌÜ 0 is finite, they define a scheme that has the closure of the set-theoretic image as underlying set.
It turns out that resultants are often conveniently described as determi- nants. Before coming to this, we explain a general context in which these determinants arise.
V.1.3   Fitting Ideals and Fitting Images There is, at least in a restricted context, an alternative notion of the image of a morphismŌÜ:X‚ÜíYof schemes, which we will call theFitting image and denote byŌÜ Fitt (X). This has the virtue that it does commute with base change, but the defect that (since, as we will see below, the Fitting imageŌÜ Fitt (X) may properly contain the scheme-theoretic image  ŐĄŌÜ(X)) it does not have the push-pull property.
Fitting Ideals.To set this up,  we need  the sheaf-theoretic version of Fitting‚Äôs lemma. LetXbe a scheme, letFbe a coherent sheaf onX,and let E 1 ő® ‚ąí‚ÜíE 0 ‚ąí‚ÜíF‚ąí‚Üí0 be an exact sequence withE 0 ‚ąľ = O n X andE 1 ‚ąľ = O m X free sheaves. (Here we allowmto be infinite.) This is independent of the choice of isomorphismsE 0 ‚ąľ = O n X andE 1 ‚ąľ = O m X , and can also be defined when E 1 andE 0 are merely quasicoherent, since it can also be described as the image of the natural map l - E 1 ‚äó l - E ‚ąó 0 ‚ąí‚ÜíO X induced by ő®. The key fact about the idealsI l (ő®) is Fitting‚Äôs Lemma. See Eisenbud [1995, page 497] for a proof.

It is instructive to compare the geometry of the zeroth Fitting ideal with the more naive notion of thesupportof a sheafF.Briefly, for any coherent sheafFon a schemeXwe define theannihilatorannF‚äāO X by taking (annF)(U)‚äāO X (U) to be the annihilator ofF(U)asanO X (U)-module for each open setU‚äāX.We define thesupportofF,denoted supp(F), to be the zero locusV(annF)‚äāXof the annihilator as a subscheme ofX.
(Note that ‚Äúsupport‚ÄĚ is used in two different senses: the support of a scheme is its underlying set or topological space, while the support of a sheaf is a scheme. The difference lies in the scheme structure: the zeroth Fitting ideal may be  properly  contained in  the  annihilator  ofF,and  the  zeroth  Fitting scheme correspondingly may properly contain the support ofF.
For  example, consider the  schemeX=A 1 K =SpecK[x],  with  sub- schemesY=V(x) andZ=V(x 2 ).  222V.  Local Constructions V.2   Resultants V.2.1   Definition of the Resultant The oldest ‚ÄĒ and still one of the most important ‚ÄĒ applications of the ideas we have introduced in the last two subsections is theresultant of two poly- nomials in one variable. To define this, say we are given two polynomials f(x)=a 0 x m +¬∑¬∑¬∑+a m , g(x)=b 0 x n +¬∑¬∑¬∑+b n , with coefficientsa i ,b i in a fieldK.The goal was to describe the condition on the coefficientsa i ,b i for the two polynomials to have a common factor; that is, a common root in the algebraic closure ofK.In applications it is also natural to look at families of polynomials. That is, we may take the coefficientsa i ,b i to be regular functions on a base schemeB,so that we think offandgas ‚Äúfamilies of polynomials in one variable parametrized byB‚ÄĚ, and we wish to describe the locus inBover whichfandghave a common factor. More precisely, we want to describe the image inB‚ÄĒin whichever sense! ‚ÄĒ of the schemeV(f, g)‚äāA 1 B
.
There are, roughly speaking, four ways to interpret this problem: we could ask formulas, in terms of thea i ‚Äôs andb i ‚Äôs, for functions generating the ideal of (1)  thereduced imageofV(f, g) ‚ÄĒ that is, the reduced scheme associated to the scheme-theoretic image; (2)  the scheme-theoretic image ofV(f, g); (3)  the Fitting image ofV(f, g); or (4)  the pullback of the image from a suitable universal family.
As shown by examples such as V-6 and V-7 above, no such formula can exist for the images in the first two senses. We shall begin by describing the classical approach, and ultimately show that (for the correct choice of ‚Äúuniversal family‚ÄĚ) it coincides with the third and fourth options, and commutes with base change.
To carry out the classical approach, we begin by choosing a universal family. We will work not over a field but over an arbitrary ringS. LetA be the polynomial ring A=S[a 0 ,...,a m ,b 0
...,b n ].
Withfandgdefined as above, we set X:=V(f, g)‚äāA 1 A and let ŌÜ:X‚äāA 1 A =SpecA√ó S A 1 S ‚ąí‚ÜíSpecA=:Y.
 V.2 Resultants223 SinceXis defined by two polynomials, we might expect it to have codi- mension 2 inA 1 A
. In this case, assuming that the map is ‚Äúgenerically finite‚ÄĚ (that is, finite over some open set) its image would have codimension 1 in SpecA. In the classical situation, whereSis field,Ahas unique factoriza- tion, so the closure of the image would be described by one equation, called theresultantoffandg.
It turns out that the conclusion of this suggestive argument is correct.
We will see in due course that the scheme-theoretic image   ŐĄŌÜ(X)ofŌÜis reduced, and coincides with the Fitting image; so that in the case of this universal family there is no ambiguity about what is meant by the image ofX.
Perhaps the most direct way to write down this equation is the following.
Consider the ring  ŐÉ A‚äāA[a ‚ąí1 0 ,b ‚ąí1 0 ] defined as  ŐÉ A=A  a 1 a 0 , ..., a m a 0 , b 1 b 0 , ..., b n b 0  
.
Let B=  ŐÉ A[őĪ 1 ,...,őĪ m ,ő≤ 1 ,...,ő≤ n ]/I, whereIis the ideal generated by them+nelements (‚ąí1) i ŌÉ i (őĪ)‚ąí a i a 0 and   (‚ąí1) i ŌÉ i (ő≤)‚ąí b i b 0 , ŌÉ i being thei-th elementary symmetric function. We may describeBintu- itively as the ring obtained fromAby adjoining the roots of the polynomials fandg. Note that  ŐÉ A‚äāBbecause the elementary symmetric functions are algebraically independent.
The expression R=  i,j (őĪ i ‚ąíő≤ j ) is a symmetric function in theőĪ i and separately in theő≤ j
. Since (‚ąí1) i a i /a 0 is  thei-th  elementary  symmetric  function  in  theőĪ i ,  and  similarly  for (‚ąí1) j b j /b 0 and theő≤ j , the functionRcan be written as a polynomial in the ratiosa i /a 0 andb j /b 0 , and thusRis an element of  ŐÉ A. EachőĪ i occursntimes inRand once in an elementary symmetric function, and similarly for theő≤‚Äôs, soRwill be bihomogeneous of degreesn, min the a i /a 0 andb j /b 0 respectively. Thus R m,n (f, g):=a n 0 b m 0 R=a n 0 b m 0  i,j (őĪ i ‚ąíő≤ j ), is inA; it is a bihomogeneous polynomial, of degreenin thea i and of degreemin theb j , called theresultantoffandg.
In general, iff 0 andg 0 are polynomial of degreesmandnover anS- algebraS 0 , we writeR m,n (f 0 ,g 0 )‚ąąS 0 for the result of substituting the 
224V.  Local Constructions coefficients off 0 andg 0 intoR m,n (f, g) ‚ÄĒ that is, the image ofR m,n (f, g) under the homomomorphism A=S[a 0 ,...,a m ,b 0 ,...,b n ]‚ÜíS 0 sending thea i andb j to the coefficients offandg. We callR m,n (f 0 ,g 0 ) theresultantoff 0 andg 0
. When we viewS 0 as an algebra over different ringsS, the resultant does not depend onS; in particular, we would have obtained the same result for anyS 0 by takingS=Z. Thus, for each mandn, we can speak of ‚Äúthe resultant‚ÄĚ as an elementR m,n of the ring Z[a 0 ,...,a m ,b 0 ,...,b n ], and ‚Äúthe resultant of two polynomials‚ÄĚf, g‚ąąS[x] as the image ofR m,n under the corresponding homomomorphism Z[a 0 ,...,a m ,b 0 ,...,b n ]‚ÜíS.
If the leading coefficients off 0 andg 0 do not vanish ‚ÄĒ that is, if these polynomials really have the stated degrees ‚ÄĒ thenR m,n (f 0 ,g 0 ) = 0 if and only iff 0 andg 0 have a common factor, and the classical goal is fulfilled.
We shall see that this even works if at most one of the leading coefficients vanish; but it turns out thatR m,n (f, g) is contained in the ideal (a 0 ,b 0 ), and thus vanishes identically on pairs of polynomials where both these coefficients are 0. In fact, the mapŌÜis not finite in this case, since over the origin inA m+n+2 S the fiber is the affine line, and the set-theoretic image of Xis not closed.
V.2.2   Sylvester‚Äôs Determinant We will next relate the zero locus of the resultant of two polynomials to the Fitting image of their common zero locus. We start by computing the equation of the Fitting imageŌÜ Fitt (X) where defined. To do this, letSbe any ring andf, g‚ąąS[x] two polynomials, of degreesmandnrespectively.
In order to define the  Fitting image ofX=V(f, g)‚äāA 1 S under the projection mapŌÄ:A 1 S ‚ÜíSpecS, we must assume that this map is finite, or equivalently that the ideal (f, g)‚äāS[x] contains a monic polynomial; see Eisenbud [1995, Proposition 4.1]. For simplicity, we assume thatfitself is monic.
To compute the ideal Fitt 0 ŌÄ ‚ąó (O X ) defining the Fitting image ofX,we first realizeŌÄ ‚ąó (O X ) as theS-moduleS[x]/(f, g). This module is the cokernel of the map S[x]‚äēS[x] (f,g) ‚ąí‚ÜíS[x] and the source and target may both be regarded as (infinitely generated) freeS-modules. But in order to compute the Fitting ideal we need a finite presentation. Since we have assumed thatfis monic of degreem, theS- submodule S[x] <m =S‚äēSx‚äēSx 2 ‚äē¬∑¬∑¬∑‚äēSx m‚ąí1 ‚äāS[x] 
V.2 Resultants225 maps ontoS[x]/(f, g); and sinceS[x] <m is isomorphic asS-module to the S-algebraS[x]/(f), we can also realize the moduleS[x]/(f, g) as the cok- ernel of the map induced by multiplication byg: G:S m ‚ąľ = S[x] <m ‚ąľ = S[x]/(f) √óg ‚ąí‚ÜíS[x]/(f) ‚ąľ = S[x] <m ‚ąľ = S m
.
Thus the Fitting ideal Fitt 0 ŌÄ ‚ąó (O X ) is generated by the determinant of an m√ómmatrix representative of the mapG:S m ‚ÜíS m
.
Now, it is not hard to write down such a matrix representative ofG explicitly, and thus to give an explicit formula for the Fitting image. But if we use the freedom we have to compute the Fitting ideal fromanyfree presentation, we can get a picture that preserves more of the symmetry betweenfandg.
To carry this out, consider the free module S[x] <m+n =S‚äēSx‚äē¬∑¬∑¬∑‚äēSx m+n‚ąí1 ‚ąľ = S m+n
.
It clearly maps ontoS[x]/(f, g), and the kernel of this map is the set of polynomialsh‚ąąS[x] of degree at mostm+n‚ąí1 that lie in the ideal (f, g)‚äā S[x]. We claim that any suchh‚ąąS[x] <m+n ‚ą©(f, g) can be expressed as a linear combination h=a¬∑f+b¬∑g for some polynomialsaof degree at mostn‚ąí1 andbof degree at most m‚ąí1. 230V.  Local Constructions V.3   Singular Schemes and Discriminants V.3.1   Definitions In this section we will take up the smooth and singluar points of a morphism of schemes. To motivate our definition, consider a mapf:M‚ÜíNof differentiable manifolds, and assume that dimM‚Č•dimN. The simplest behavior of such a map occurs at pointsx‚ąąMwhere the differentialDf x is surjective: restricted to a suitable neighborhoodUof such a pointx, the mapflooks like the projection onto one factor of a product, and we say thatxis a smooth point off.
In the category of schemes, the Zariski open sets are too large to per- mit product structures, and (as we shall see) there are also complications arising from the fact that points may have different residue fileds. It is still possible to define smoothness of a morphism of schemes in terms of local product structure ‚ÄĒ see for example Altman and Kleiman [1970] ‚ÄĒ but in the present context it will make more sense to adopt a characterization of smooth and singular points that generalizes the differential characterization for manifolds.
To carry this out we introduce the module of K Őąahler differentials of a homomomorphism of rings, and its global version, the relative cotangent sheaf of a morphism of schemes. IfŌą:A‚ÜíBis a map of rings, we define ‚Ą¶ B/A , themodule ofA-linear K Őąahler differentials, to be the freeB-module generated by symbolsdbfor allb‚ąąB, modulo the relations d(b 1 b 2 )=b 1 db 2 +b 2 db 1 for allb 1 ,b 2 ‚ąąB and dŌą a = 0   for alla‚ąąA.
These relations ensure that the map B‚ąí‚Üí‚Ą¶ B/A , b‚ąí‚Üídb, is anA-linear derivation; in fact, it is the universalA-linear derivation in a suitable sense. (See Eisenbud [1995] for details.) It is easy to deduce from this, for example, that ifB=A[x 1 ,...,x n ]/(f 1 ,...,f n ) then ‚Ą¶ B/A in the cokernel of the Jacobian matrix  ‚ąāf i ‚ąāx j 
.
To globalize, letŌÜ:X‚ÜíYbe a morphism of schemes. We define the relative cotangent sheafofŌÜ:X‚ÜíY,written ‚Ą¶ X/Y , to be the sheaf whose value on an open affine subsetUofXmapping to an open affine subset VofYis the module ofO Y (V)-linear K Őąahler differentials ofO X (U). The collection of open sets inXjust specified forms a baseBfor the open sets ofX.The axioms for aB-sheaf (see Section I.1.3) are easily checked ‚ÄĒ they 
V.3 Singular Schemes and Discriminants231 amount to the statment that the construction of modules of differentials is compatible with localization ‚ÄĒ so the data just given really does define a sheaf.
Therelative dimensionof a morphismŌÜ:X‚ÜíYof schemes at a point x‚ąąXis defined to be the difference dim(X, x)‚ąídim(Y, ŌÜ(x)).
Definition V-21.LetŌÜ:X‚ÜíYbe a morphism of Noetherian schemes, and suppose thatŌÜis flat, of finite type, and has constant relative codimen- siond. We define thesingular schemesingŌÜ‚äāXofŌÜto be the subscheme ofXdefined by thed-th Fitting ideal of the relative cotangent sheaf ofŌÜ; that is, singŌÜ=V(Fitt d ‚Ą¶ X/Y )‚äāX.
In case the morphismŌÜ| singŌÜ : singŌÜ‚ÜíYis finite, we define thedis- criminant scheme‚ąÜ(ŌÜ)‚äāYto be the Fitting image of singŌÜinY,that is, ‚ąÜ(ŌÜ)=V(Fitt 0 (ŌÜ ‚ąó O singŌÜ ))‚äāY.
To understand this definition, we first return to the most classical case, that of a mapŌÜ:X‚ÜíYof irreducible, nonsingular varieties over an algebraically closed field. In this case the condition of constant relative dimension is automatic, while flatness translates into the condition that the dimension of the fibersŌÜ ‚ąí1 (y) is constant, and equal to the relative dimension.
The notion of a singular scheme being a local one, we may pass to the affine case and assume thatY=SpecAand write X=SpecA[x 1 ,...,x n ]/(f 1 ,...,f m ).
As we noted, in this case the module ofA-linear K Őąahler differentials ‚Ą¶ X/Y is the cokernel of then√ómJacobian matrix Ô£ę Ô£¨ Ô£≠ ‚ąāf 1 /‚ąāx 1

However, the scheme-theoretic setting presents many new phenomena.
For example, consider a finite extension of fieldsK‚ÜíK  , and let ŌÜ:X=SpecK  ‚ąí‚ÜíY=SpecK be the corresponding morphism of one-point schemes.
