 232V.  Local Constructions V.3.2   Discriminants We now want to consider in more detail the case of a morphismŌÜ:X‚Üí Ywhere the singular scheme singŌÜis finite overY, and to describe the discriminant scheme ‚ąÜ(ŌÜ)‚äāYassociated to such a map. We will focus primarily (but not exclusively) on the case whereXis a closed subscheme of A 1 Y , given as the zero locus of a single polynomialf(x) whose coefficients are regular functions onY; this will lead us to the definition of thediscriminant of a polynomial in onediscriminant!of a polynomialvariable. In this setting, by analogy with the discussion of resultants in the preceding section, we may view the problem of defining the discriminant scheme as that of giving formulas for the equations defining the set of pointsy=[p]‚ąąY=SpecA over which the polynomialfhas repeated factors, that is, such that the reduction  ŐĄ f‚ąąA/p[x]off‚ąąA[x]modphas multiple roots in the algebraic closure ofőļ(y). As in the discussion of resultants, we will have both the general definition above using Fitting ideals, and (in the restricted case X=SpecA[x]/(f)) a more classical notion of discriminant defined in effect as the pullback of the (reduced) branch scheme of a suitable ‚Äúuniversal family‚ÄĚ of polynomials; and we will ultimately show that they coincide where the latter is defined.
To set up the classical construction, we need first of all to define our universal branched cover. Let A=Z[a 0 ,...,a m ], and let f=a 0 x m +¬∑¬∑¬∑+a m ‚ąąA[x] be the generic polynomial in one variable of degreem. As in the proof of Theorem V-15, these coefficients are algebraically independent, and we regardAas a subring ofB.
InBwe may form the polynomial D 1 =  i<j (őĪ i ‚ąíőĪ j ).
This polynomial vanishes when two of the roots offare equal, but it is not a solution of our problem because it is not a polynomial inA. For one thing, it is not invariant under permutations of the roots. But it is easy to see that ifŌÄis a permutation of the roots,D 1 applied to the permuted roots is equal to sgn(ŌÄ)D 1 , where sgn(ŌÄ)=¬Ī1 is the sign of the permutation. Thus D 2 1 is invariant under under permutations of the roots, and is expressible as a polynomial in the symmetric functionsŌÉ i (őĪ)=a i /a 0
. EachőĪ i occurs

Proposition V-22.With notation as above, we have D m (f)= (‚ąí1) m(m‚ąí1)/2 a 0 R m,m‚ąí1 (f, f  ).

Proof.Applying Corollary V-17 to the ringBdefined above we see that R m,m‚ąí1 (f, f  )=a m‚ąí1 0 m  i=1 f  (őĪ i ).

The second statement of the corollary fol- lows from the fact that the resultant of a monic polynomial and another polynomial over a field vanishes if and only if the two have a common root, and the usual computation that shows, over a field, that a polynomialf(x) and its derivative have a common root if and only iffhas a multiple root.
As a corollary of this proposition, we have the promised identity be- tween the  classical and modern definitions, in the restricted caseX= SpecA[x]/(f).

V.3.3   Examples To illustrate our definition of the discriminant scheme of a morphism, we will calculate it in a number of specific examples. For all of the following morphismsŌÜ:X‚ÜíY,the target space will be the affine lineY=SpecK[t] over a fieldKof characteristic zero. For all but the  last example, the morphismŌÜwill be finite; and for all but the last two,Xwill be a subscheme of the affine lineA 1 Y =SpecK[t][x], finite and flat overY.Specifically, we take ŌÜ:X=SpecK[t, x]/(f)‚ÜíY=SpecK[t], where f(x)=x k +a k‚ąí1 (t)x k‚ąí1 +...+a 1 (t)x+a 0 (t) is a monic polynomial inxwith coefficients inK[t]. In all the cases we consider the discriminant scheme, being a subscheme ofA 1 K supported at the origin, is determined by its degree.

Note that in this setting the discriminant scheme measures not only the number of sheets in a branched cover that come together (that is, the nonreducedness of the special fiber, as measured by the ‚Äúk‚ąí1‚ÄĚ factor in the expressionőī k,m =m(k‚ąí1)), but also how fast they are coming together (the ‚Äúm‚ÄĚ factor). Thus, while in all the examples of the form X=SpecK[t, x]/(x 2 ‚ąít m )‚ÜíY=SpecK[t] the fiber over the origin inY is the same double point, the discriminant has different degrees depending on the speed with which the two pointsx=¬Ī ‚ąö t m approach each other as tapproaches 0.

Although the examples we have seen so far may seem special, we can use any of the three approaches to describe the discriminant of any finite flat morphismŌÜ:X‚ÜíYwhereYis nonsingular and one-dimensional andX is locally embeddable inA 1 Y , that is, for any pointp‚ąąXthe local ringO X,p is of the formO Y,ŌÜ(p) [x]/(f). For example, following the second approach we may make a base changeY  ‚ÜíYso that the polynomialffactors completely into linear factors overO Y (that is, the pullbackX  =X√ó Y Y  is a union ofkirreducible componentsX i , each mapping isomorphically toY). The discriminant of the pullback morphismŌÜ  :X  ‚ÜíY  will then be given by the product of the pairwise differences of the factors off,so that its degree will be the sum of the degrees of the pairwise intersections X i ‚ą©X j ; and the degree of the discriminant ofŌÜat each pointy‚ąąYwill be simply the sum of the degrees of the discriminant scheme ofŌÜ  at the pointsy  ofY  lying overy, each divided by the order of ramification of Y  ‚ÜíYat that point.
Our next example, accordingly, will be the simplest example of a mor- phismX‚ÜíYsuch thatXis not locally embeddable inA 1 Y
.

IfXhad consisted of threecoplanarlines meeting at a point ‚ÄĒ say, if we tookŌÜ:X=SpecK[t, x]/(x 3 ‚ąít 3 )‚ÜíY=SpecK[t] ‚ÄĒ then by the calculation made previously we would have deg(‚ąÜ(ŌÜ)) = 6, while in the 
V.3 Singular Schemes and Discriminants239 spatial case we have deg(‚ąÜ(ŌÜ)) = 4. This difference is what underlies the example in Section II.3.4 whose general fiber is isomorphic to the three co- ordinate lines inA 3 K and whose special fiber is supported on three coplanar lines: the jump in the discriminant, in a sense, forces the appearance of the embedded point in the flat limit. We saw the same phenomenon emerge when we considered Hilbert polynomials in Sections III.3.1 and III.3.2.

In the preceding exercise, the singular curves of the families considered have the simplest possible curve singularity, called anode. To generalize it, we need first of all to make a definition: Definition V-31.LetC‚ÜíSpecKbe a curve over an algebraically closed fieldKandp‚ąąCa closed point ofC.We will say thatpis anode,cuspor tacnodeofCif the formal completion ňÜ Oof the local ringO C,p with respect to its maximal ideal is isomorphic toK[[x, y]]/(y 2 ‚ąíx 2 ),K[[x, y]]/(y 2 ‚ąíx 3 ) orK[[x, y]]/(y 2 ‚ąíx 4 ) respectively.
These singularities may also be characterized geometrically: a node, for example, is a point ofCat which two smooth branches cross transversely; a tacnode is one where two smooth branches are simply tangent, that is, intersecting in a scheme of degree 2. In the case of a plane curveC‚äāP 2 K , we will say that a node or tacnodepofCisordinaryif neither branch indi- vidually has intersection multiplicity 3 or more with its projective tangent line. (The reader may verify that ifp‚ąąCis a cusp, this will always be the case.)  240V.  Local Constructions V.4   Dual Curves V.4.1   Definitions We consider now another object associated to a plane curveC‚äāP 2 S : its dual curveC ‚ąó , a subscheme of the dual projective plane (P 2 S ) ‚ąó as defined in Section III.2.3. In classical algebraic geometry, the definition is simple enough: forKan algebraically closed field andC‚äāP 2 K nonsingular of degreed‚Č•2, the dual curve is defined to be the set of projective tangent linesT p CtoC,regarded as points of the dual projective plane (P 2 K ) ‚ąó
. More generally, for a curveCwithout multiple components and containing no lines the dual curve is defined to be the closure of the locus of tangent lines T p CtoCat nonsingular pointspofC.(In the following, we will refer to this locus as the ‚Äúclassical dual‚ÄĚ.) As in the case of flexes (Section IV.1), what we will do here is to propose a natural set of defining equations forC ‚ąó , which will yield a definition of the dualC ‚ąó ‚äā(P 2 S ) ‚ąó of a plane curveC‚äāP 2 S over an arbitrary scheme S.Also as in the case of flexes,C ‚ąó will be a closed subscheme of (P 2 S ) ‚ąó , flat overSover the open subset ofSwhere it does not contain the fiber of (P 2 S ) ‚ąó , and invariant under base change. Our definition will agree with the classical definition for nonsingular plane curves over fields of characteristic zero, but not for singular ones: ifpis an isolated singular point of a plane curveC‚äāP 2 K , the line in (P 2 K ) ‚ąó dual topwill be a component ofC ‚ąó in our definition, though it is not part of the classical dual. Again, this is necessary if we want the duals of a family of plane curves to form a closed family; if we want to recover information about the classical dual, we simply discard the extra components. (The need for characteristic zero will be explained below.) To make our definition, we have first to introduce one auxiliary object.
Recall from Section III.2.8 that in the productP 2 S √ó S (P 2 S ) ‚ąó we have the universal lineő£, whose fiber over each pointl‚ąą(P 2 S ) ‚ąó is the corresponding linel‚äāP 2 S : in terms of homogeneous coordinatesX, Y, ZonP 2 S andA, B, C on (P 2 S ) ‚ąó , ő£=V(AX+BY+CZ)‚äāP 2 S √ó S (P 2 S ) ‚ąó
.
Now letC‚äāP 2 S be any plane curve, and assume for the moment thatC contains no lines (that is, there is no points‚ąąSand linel‚äāP 2 őļ(s) in the fiber ofP 2 S overscontained inC; this is stronger than supposing that the equation ofCdoes not have a linear factor). We define theuniversal line sectionőď C ofCto be the intersection őď C =ŌÄ ‚ąí1 1 (C)‚ą©ő£‚äāP 2 S √ó S  (P 2 S ) ‚ąó
.
By our hypothesis thatCcontains no lines, the mapŌÄ 2 :őď C ‚Üí(P 2 S ) ‚ąó will be finite (and hence flat) of degreed; and we define thedual curveC ‚ąó ‚äā (P 2 S ) ‚ąó ofCto be the discriminant scheme of this map, as in Definition V-21.
 V.4 Dual Curves241 Finally, ifCdoes contain lines, we simply defineC ‚ąó to be the closure in (P 2 S ) ‚ąó of the discriminant scheme of the restriction ofŌÄ 2 to the open subset of (P 2 S ) ‚ąó where it is finite.
A few introductory remarks are in order. To begin with,C ‚ąó is by defi- nition a closed subscheme of (P 2 S ) ‚ąó , and its formation commutes with base change: ifS  ‚ÜíSis any morphism andC  =S  √ó S C‚äāP 2 S  , then we will have also őď C  =S  √ó S őď C ‚äāP 2 S  √ó S (P 2 S  ) ‚ąó , and henceC  ‚ąó =S  √ó S C ‚ąó
. In particular, for any points‚ąąS,the fiberC ‚ąó s ofC ‚ąó overswill be the dual of the fiberC s ‚äāP 2 őļ(s) ofCovers.
The support ofC ‚ąó is easy to describe: by the definition of the discrimi- nant scheme of a finite morphism, it is the set of linesl‚ąą(P 2 S ) ‚ąó such that the intersection of the corresponding linel‚äāP 2 S withCis singular over őļ(l). (This means eitherl‚ą©Cis nonreduced, or ‚ÄĒ in caseőļ(l) has charac- teristicp>0 and is not algebraically closed ‚ÄĒ has a point whose residue field in an inseparable extension ofőļ(l).) In particular, as we indicated, if p‚ąąC‚äāP 2 K is any singular point, the line in (P 2 K ) ‚ąó dual top‚ÄĒ that is, the locus of lines inP 2 K passing throughp‚ÄĒ will be contained inC ‚ąó
.
Finally, we remark that the ‚Äúdual curve‚ÄĚ need not be a curve! IfC‚äāP 2 S is nonreduced ‚ÄĒ that is, it has a multiple component ‚ÄĒ then the dualC ‚ąó will be all of (P 2 S ) ‚ąó
.EvenifC‚äāP 2 S is nonsingular and reduced it may have nonreduced fibersC s over some points ofS.For example, the fiber of the curveC=V(X 2 +Y 2 +Z 2 )‚äāP 2 Z over (2)‚ąąSpecZis a double line. In such cases the dualC ‚ąó will contain the corresponding fibers of (P 2 S ) ‚ąó , and so will not be a ‚Äúplane curve‚ÄĚ as we have defined it.
Exercise V-33.Verify that the dualC ‚ąó of the plane curve C=V(X 2 +Y 2 +Z 2 )‚äāP 2 Z mentioned above is, by our definition, the subscheme C ‚ąó =V(4A 2 +4B 2 +4C 2 )‚äā(P 2 Z ) ‚ąó , so is not a plane curve in (P 2 Z ) ‚ąó

In the example of Exercise V-33, our definition seems at first willfully perverse: why shouldn‚Äôt we take the dual to be the zero locus ofA 2 +B 2 +C 2 instead? Indeed, as long as the baseSis nonsingular of dimension one (as in the exercise) any fibers of (P 2 S ) ‚ąó ‚ÜíScontained inC ‚ąó will be components of C ‚ąó ; we can discard them to arrive at a scheme  ŐÉ C ‚ąó flat overS.But we will see below how to construct examples of curvesC‚äāP 2 S with nonreduced fibers C s over isolated pointss‚ąąSwhere this simply cannot be avoided: the fiber (P 2 őļ(s) ) ‚ąó of (P 2 S ) ‚ąó overswill be contained in the closure ofC ‚ąó ‚ą©(P 2 S\{s} ) ‚ąó
.
If we want the definition to behave well with respect to base change, this means the dual ofC s has to be all of (P 2 őļ(s) ) ‚ąó
.
 242V.  Local Constructions Exercise V-34.LetKbe a field, andC‚äāP 2 K a curve, smooth over K.Show that the dualC ‚ąó is reduced, unless every tangent line toCis multiply tangent ‚ÄĒ that is, has intersection multiplicity 3 or more withC, or is tangent toCat more than one point.
In fact, if the characteristic ofKis zero it cannot happen that every tangent line is multiply tangent (see for example Harris [1995, Proposition 15.3]), so the dual of any nonsingular plane curve will be reduced. But there are examples of plane curves over fields of finite characteristic such that every point is a flex, and other examples where every tangent line is tangent at several points. In such a case, our definition yields a nonreduced dual curveC ‚ąó
.

V.4.3   Curves with Multiple Components As in the case of flexes (Section IV.1), a very different sort of question emerges when we consider curves with multiple components. Here, as we have said, the definition of the dual curveC ‚ąó yields not a curve in (P 2 K ) ‚ąó , but rather the whole dual plane (P 2 K ) ‚ąó
. But we can consider a family of 
V.4 Dual Curves243 generically nonsingular curves specializing to a multiple curve and ask: in such a family, where do the tangent lines go?
We will illustrate this problem with the same sort of curve as we used in Section IV.1; but we will start with a simpler example: instead of a family of quartics specializing to a double conic we will consider a family of conics degenerating to a double line. To set it up, letKbe an algebraically closed field of characteristic zero, and setS=SpecK[t]. LetL(X, Y, Z) and Q(X, Y, Z)‚ąąK[X, Y, Z] be homogeneous polynomials of degrees 1 and 2 respectively, and assume that their zero loci inP 2 K intersect transversely, that is, thatV(Q, L)‚äāP 2 K is reduced. Consider the curveC‚äāP 2 S given by ŌÄ:C= Proj(K[t])[X, Y, Z]/(L(X, Y, Z) 2 +tQ(X, Y, Z)) ‚äāProj(K[t])[X, Y, Z]=P 2 S ‚ÜíS.
LetC ‚ąó ‚äā(P 2 S ) ‚ąó be the dual of the curveC‚äāP 2 S
. As with the scheme of flexes of the curveC,the schemeC ‚ąó will have two components: one, the fiber of (P 2 S ) ‚ąó over the origin (t)‚ąąS; and the other the closureC  of the inverse image inC ‚ąó of the punctured lineT=SpecK[t, t ‚ąí1 ]=S\{(t)}‚äāS (equivalently, the closure of the dual of the curveC‚ą©P 2 T ‚äāP 2 T ). The scheme C  will be flat over all ofS,with fiber over a point (t‚ąíőľ)‚ąąSother than the origin the dual (C őľ ) ‚ąó of the curveC őľ =V(F+őľG)‚äāP 2 K ; it will therefore have as fiber over the origin a schemeC  0 ‚äāP 2 K of dimension 1 and degree 2, which we will call the ‚Äúlimiting position‚ÄĚ of the duals of the nearby nonsingular curvesC őľ asőľapproaches zero. Once more, we can translate the naive question, ‚Äúwhere do the tangent lines to a conic go when the conic degenerates into a double line?‚ÄĚ into the precise problem: determine the support of the flat limitC  0
. The answer is expressed in the following Proposition V-37.The fiberC  0 ofC  over the origin(t)‚ąąSconsists of the union of the two lines dual to the two pointst=L=Q=0of intersection of the lineL=0and the conicQ=0in the planet=0.
Proof.We will do this by explicit calculation. To begin with, since the characteristic ofKis not 2, we can choose affine coordinates (x, y) on the plane so that the lineV(L) is thex-axisy= 0, and the conicV(Q) (if it is nonsingular) the zero locus ofy 2 ‚ąíx 2 + 1. V.5   Double Point Loci LetŌÜ:X‚ÜíYbe a morphism of varieties. Thedouble point locusofŌÜwas classically defined to be the closure inX√óXof the locus of pairs of distinct pointsp, q‚ąąXwith common imageŌÜ(p)=ŌÜ(q)‚ąąY.In this section we will give a scheme-theoretic definition that, as we will see, captures more of the geometry of the map.
For the following, we will letŌÜ:X‚ÜíYbe a separated morphism of schemes. As we saw in Chapter III, the separated hypothesis means that the diagonal ‚ąÜ X is a closed subscheme of the fiber productX√ó Y X,and is satisfied for all affine and projective schemes.
Definition V-41.Thedouble point schemeD ŌÜ of a separated morphism ŌÜ:X‚ÜíYis the scheme D ŌÜ =V(annI ‚ąÜ X )‚äāX√ó Y X associated to the ideal annI ‚ąÜ X ‚äāO X√ó Y X of functionsfwithfI ‚ąÜ X =0.
To understand this definition, recall that ifŌÜ:X‚ÜíYis a morphism ofS-schemes (in the examples belowSwill be SpecKor SpecZ) and Y‚ÜíSis separated, then the fiber productX√ó Y Xis a closed subscheme ofX√ó S X: it is the (scheme-theoretic) inverse image X√ó Y X=(ŌÜ√óŌÜ) ‚ąí1 (‚ąÜ Y ) 
V.5 Double Point Loci247 where ‚ąÜ Y ‚äāY√ó S Yis the diagonal. (Note that we have a fiber square X√ó Y X  Y X√ó S X   Y√ó S Y; ‚ąÜ  that is,X√ó Y Xis the fibered product ofX√ó S XandYoverY√ó S Y.) Now, away from the diagonal, the double point schemeD ŌÜ is simply the fiber productX√ó Y X; in particular, the closed points of its support away from the diagonal are simply the pairs of distinct pointsp, q‚ąąXsuch thatŌÜ(p)=ŌÜ(q). In this sense it generalizes the classical double point locus, though it may have nontrivial scheme structure even in caseŌÜis a morphism of varieties. In addition, as our examples will show, it may have components supported on the diagonal ‚ąÜ X ‚äāX√ó S X‚ÄĒ that is, its support may properly contain the double point locus ‚ÄĒ and may even be nonempty in cases where the classical double point locus is empty.
Example V-42.Consider the maps fromX=A 1 K =SpecK[t]toY= A 2 K =SpecK[x, y] given by polynomials of degree 3, mappingXonto plane cubic curves with a node or a cusp. To see the difference between the classical and modern approaches, ob- serve that forőĽ= 0 the schemeX√ó Y X, as pictured in the figure below, is reduced; hence the double point scheme is reduced as well and coincides with the classical double point locus. ForőĽ= 0, however, they differ: the schemeX√ó Y Xconsists of the diagonal inX√ó K X,plus an embedded point supported at the origin. In the classical language, we see only the reduced scheme associated toX√ó Y X,and so miss the embedded point; thus the double point locus ofŌÜ 0 is empty. Scheme theory, by contrast, does see the nonreduced structure ofX√ó Y Xand as a result the double point scheme ofŌÜ 0 is nonempty, reflecting the fact thatŌÜ 0 fails to be an immersion att=0.

 VI Schemes and Functors At the end of the first chapter of this book we discussed a way of embedding the category of schemes into the larger category of contravariant functors Ffrom the category of schemes to the category of sets.
This embedding is useful in at least three ways: (1)  The effect of some basic constructions, such as products, is much easier to describe on functors of points than on schemes.
(2)  In trying to construct a certain scheme, it is often easy to construct the  functor that  would be the  functor  of points of  that scheme, if the scheme existed; the construction problem is then reduced to the problem of proving that the functor is representable and the use of Yoneda‚Äôs Lemma (VI-1). The process is exactly analogous to the use of distributions in analysis: there, when trying to prove the existence of a nice function solving a given differential equation, one first proves the existence of a solution that is a distribution, and then is left with the (possibly more tractable)regularityproblem of proving that the distribution is represented by integration against a function.
(3)  Many aspects of the geometry of schemes can be extended to the cat- egory of functors, so that it is sometimes useful to forget about repre- senting a functor and work in that category (or some suitable subcat- egory) directly.
In this chapter we illustrate these points, first with some basic construc- tions, and then with some examples coming from the desire to parametrize families of schemes. We shall see in Section VI.2.4 that some of these lead to functors that are not actually schemes, though they are rather close.
 252VI.  Schemes and Functors VI.1   The Functor of Points We start where we left off at the end of Chapter I. Recall that thefunctor of pointsof a schemeXis the functor h X : (schemes) ‚ó¶ ‚Üí(sets) where (schemes) ‚ó¶ and (sets) represent the category of schemes with the arrows reversed and the category of sets;h X takes each schemeYto the set h X (Y) = Mor(Y, X) and each morphismf:Y‚ÜíZto the map of sets h X (Z)‚Üíh X (Y) defined by sending an elementg‚ąąh X (Z) = Mor(Z, X) to the composition g‚ó¶f‚ąąMor(Y, X). We say that a functorF:  (schemes) ‚ó¶ ‚Üí(sets) is representableif it is of the formh X for some schemeX.By Yoneda‚Äôs Lemma below,Xis unique if it exists; in this case we say thatXrepresentsF.
The seth X (Y) is called the set ofY-valued points ofX(ifY=SpecTis affine, we will often writeh X (T) instead ofh X (SpecT) and call it the set ofT-valued points ofX).
Recall also that this construction defines a functor h: (schemes)‚ÜíFun((schemes) ‚ó¶ ,(sets)) (where morphisms in the category of functors are natural transformations), sending X‚Üíh X and associating to a morphismf:X‚ÜíX  the natural transformation h X ‚Üíh X  that for any schemeYsendsg‚ąąh X (Y) = Mor(Y, X) to the compositionf‚ó¶g‚ąąh X  (Y) = Mor(Y, X  ).
In order for this notion to be of any use at all, a crucial first fact is that the functor of pointsh X really does determine the schemeX.This follows from a basic categorical fact.
Lemma VI-1(Yoneda‚Äôs Lemma).LetCbe a category and letX, X  be objects ofC.
(a)IfFis any contravariant functor fromCto the category of sets, the natural transformations fromMor(‚ąí,X)toFare in natural correspon- dence with the elements ofF(X).
(b)If the functorsMor(‚ąí,X)andMor(‚ąí,X  )fromCto the category of sets are isomorphic, thenXX 
. More generally, the maps of functors fromMor(‚ąí,X)toMor(‚ąí,X  )are the same as maps fromXtoX  ; that is, the functor h:C‚ÜíFun(C ‚ó¶ ,(sets)) 
VI.1 The Functor of Points253 sendingXtoh X is an equivalence ofCwith a full subcategory of the category of functors.
Proof.For part (a), the correspondence sendsőĪ: Mor(‚ąí,X)‚ÜíFto the elementőĪ(1 X ), where 1 X :X‚ÜíXis the identity map. The inverse takes p‚ąąF(X) to the mapőĪsending an elementf‚ąąMor(Y, X)toF(f)(p)‚ąą F(Y). As for part (b), we can apply the statement of part (a) to the functor F= Mor(‚ąí,Y).
The following improvement of Lemma VI-1 shows that it is enough to look at the functor of points restricted to the category of affine schemes, or, equivalently, to the category (rings) ‚ó¶ , the category of commutative rings with the arrows reversed; and the same thing works in the relative setting.
Proposition VI-2.IfRis a commutative ring, a scheme overRis de- termined by the restriction of its functor of points to affine schemes over R; in fact h:(R-schemes)‚ÜíFun((R-algebras),(sets)) is an equivalence of the category ofR-schemes with a full subcategory of the category of functors.
Of course, a contravariant functor on the category of affine schemes is the same as a covariant functor on the category of rings; so given this result, we will generally think of our contravariant representable functors h X : (schemes) ‚ó¶ ‚Üí(sets) as covariant functors onR-algebras. (If we need to make a distinction, we will denote byh ‚ąó X : (rings)‚Üí(sets) the functor defined byh ‚ąó X (A)=h X (SpecA) for anyR-algebraA.) Proof.This is really just the statement that schemes are built up out of affine schemes. LetS=SpecR. Writeh X for the functor Mor S (‚ąí,X) re- stricted to the category of affine schemes overS.It is enough to show that any natural transformationŌÜ:h X ‚Üíh X  comes from a unique morphism foverSfromXtoX 
. To constructffromŌÜ, let{U a }be an affine cover ofX,and applyŌÜto the inclusion mapsU a ‚äāXto get morphisms U a ‚ÜíX 
. These morphisms satisfy the compatibility conditions necessary to define the desired morphismf. Uniqueness comes down to the obser- vation that two morphisms fromXtoX  that differ are already different when restricted to one of theU a

 254VI.  Schemes and Functors VI.1.1   Open and Closed Subfunctors One reason that thinking of schemes as functors is useful is that it is pos- sible to extend some of the basic notions from the geometry of schemes to functors. We will consider some examples.
We first show how to define an open subfunctor of a functor F‚ąąFun((rings),(sets)).
We say that a mapőĪ:G‚ÜíFof functors from a categoryCto the category of sets isinjectiveif for every objectXthe induced map of setsG(X)‚Üí F(X) is injective (this corresponds to the standard categorical notion, but we will not need this fact). In this case we will say thatőĪ:G‚ÜíFis a subfunctorofF.For example, ifU‚äāXis a subscheme, the functorh U will be a subfunctor ofh X
.
We want to define an open subfunctor of a functorFto be a subfunctor that, when restricted to a representable subfunctorh X ‚äāF, is of the form h U ‚äāh X for an open subschemeU‚äāX. To carry this out, we need to introduce the notion of a fibered product of functors.
Definition VI-4.IfA,B,andCare functors from some categoryCto the category of sets and iff:A‚ÜíCandg:B‚ÜíCare morphisms of functors, thefibered productA√ó C Bis the functor fromCto (sets) defined by setting, for any objectZofC, (A√ó C B)(Z)={(a, b)‚ąąA(Z)√óB(Z)|f(a)=f(b)inC(Z)}, and defined on morphisms ofCin the obvious way.
Definition VI-5.A subfunctorőĪ:G‚ÜíFin Fun((rings),(sets)) is an open subfunctorif, for each mapŌą:h SpecR ‚ÜíFfrom the functor repre- sented by an affine scheme SpecR(that is, eachŌą‚ąąF(R)), the fibered product G Ōą  h SpecR G  őĪ  F Ōą  of functors yields a mapG Ōą ‚Üíh SpecR isomorphic to the injection from the functor represented by some open subcheme of SpecR.

We will also use the notion of anopen coveringof a functor. This is a collection of open subfunctors that yields an open covering of a scheme whenever we pull back to a representable functor. More precisely: letF: (schemes)‚Üí(sets) be a functor. Consider a collection{G i ‚ÜíF}of open subfunctors ofF. For each maph x ‚ÜíFfrom a representable functor h X toF, there are open subschemesU i ‚äāXsuch that the fiber product h X √ó F G i ofh X andG i ish U i
. We say that the collection{G i ‚ÜíF}is an open covering if, for any such maph X ‚ÜíF, the corresponding open subschemesU i ‚äāXcoverX.
One warning: if{G i ‚ÜíF}is an open covering ofF, it is not necessarily the case thatF(T)=  G i (T) for all schemesT. (For example, consider F=h SpecZ ,G 1 =h SpecZ[1/p] ,G 2 =h SpecZ[1/q] , wherepandqare distinct primes. Then{G i ‚ÜíF}is an open covering, but F(SpecZ) consists of one point (the identity map), whereasG i (SpecZ)= ‚ąÖfori=1,2.) However, it is the case that an open covering{G i ‚ÜíF}

VI.1.2K-Rational Points IfXis a scheme over a fieldK,theK-valued points ofXoverKare maps SpecK‚ÜíXwhose composition with the natural mapX‚ÜíSpecKis the identity. We claim that such maps correspond exactly to closed pointspof Xthat arerational overK(orK-rational) in the sense that the residue class fieldőļ(p)isK(via the inclusion map ofKinto the local ring ofX atp). Indeed, since SpecKhas no nontrivial open coverings, a map from SpecKintoXis a map into some affine open subscheme SpecTofX,and such a morphism is determined by aK-algebra mapT‚ÜíK‚ÄĒ that is, by a maximal ideal ofTwith residue class fieldK.Conversely, we may reverse the construction and see that anyK-rational closed pointpgives rise to a unique morphism SpecK‚ÜíXofK-schemes.
We reiterate the warning about working in the category ofS-schemes rather than the category of all schemes, where applicable. For example, when working with complex varieties, one would expecth SpecC (SpecC)to be a single point (the identity map) ‚ÄĒ and this is true in the category of C-schemes. But in the category of schemes, this set is very large!
Exercise VI-12.LetX=SpecC,considered as an abstract scheme, that is, a scheme overZ.Describe the seth X (SpecC) of allC-valued points of SpecC.
VI.1.3   Tangent Spaces to a Functor Sometimes it is much easier to compute geometric information about a scheme if one knows its functor of points than if one knows its equations!
A typical example occurs with the Zariski tangent space. (We will see this applied in Section VI.2.3).
We will work with schemes over a fixed fieldK,and all morphisms will be morphisms overK.
Recall that ifXis a scheme, then for anyK-rational pointp‚ąąXthe Zariski tangent space toXatpis Hom K (m/m 2 ,K), wherem=m X,p is the maximal ideal in the local ring ofXatpandK=őļ(p)=O X,p /m X,p is the residue field ofXatp. Now letXbe a scheme overK.LetXbe the affine scheme SpecK[őĶ]/(őĶ) 2
. We claim thataK[őĶ]/(őĶ) 2 -valued point ofX is the same as aK-rational closed pointpofXtogether with an element of the Zariski tangent space toXatp.

In Section VI.2.1 we will see how this characterization of tangent vectors to a scheme may be used to give an intrinsic description of tangent vectors to projective space, completing the discussion in Section III.2.4.

VI.1.4   Group Schemes It is extremely easy to specify extra structure on a scheme by specifying it on the functor. For example, we may define agroup schemeas a schemeG and a factorization of the functorh G (rings)‚Üí(sets) through the forget- ful functor (groups)‚Üí(sets) ‚ÄĒ that is, a group scheme is a scheme and a natural way of regarding Mor(X, G) as a group for eachX.
By Yoneda‚Äôs Lemma (VI-1), this is the same thing as giving maps G√óG‚ÜíG,    G‚ÜíGand    SpecK‚ÜíG representing the multiplication, inverse, and identity element, respectively, and satisfying the usual laws (associativity and so on), but is often much simpler. For example, GL n can be defined as the affine scheme of invertible integraln√ónmatrices, SpecZ[x ij ][det(x ij ) ‚ąí1 ], but one usually thinks of it as a functor that associates to every ringTthe group GL n (T). The interesting point here is just that this family of groups already specifies the structure of a scheme and the additional structure maps!
 VI.2 Characterization of a Space by its Functor of Points259 VI.2   Characterization of a Space by its Functor of Points It is often much easier, and sometimes more illuminating, to describe the functor of points of an interesting scheme than to give a direct construction of it, whether by gluing or by explicit equations in affine or projective space. Typical examples come from the Hilbert scheme and other moduli problems, where one wants a natural space whose points represent some geometric objects. However, the point of view is also useful in discussing much simpler objects, such as fibered products, or projective space itself.
The basic idea in any case is to first define a functor from the category of schemes to the category of sets, and then to prove an existence theorem asserting that there is a scheme of which it is the functor of points. Of course, to carry this procedure out an essential ingredient is a local (and readily verifiable) criterion for a functor to be representable, and we start by giving such a criterion.
VI.2.1   Characterization of Schemes among Functors To the extent that we want to define and/or construct schemes first as functors, we run into a fundamental problem, that of determining when a functor comes from a scheme. Here is one characterization.
We say thatF: (rings)‚Üí(sets) is asheaf in the Zariski topologyif for each ringRand each open covering ofX=SpecRby distinguished open affinesU i =SpecR f i the functorFsatisfies the sheaf axiom for the open covering  U i =X.That is, for every collection of elementsőĪ i ‚ąąF(R f i ) such thatőĪ i andőĪ j map to the same element inF(R f i f j ), there is a unique elementőĪ‚ąąF(R) mapping to each of theőĪ i
.
This is a reasonably easy property to check in practice. It is in fact enough to guarantee thatFcomes from a scheme if we know already that Fis covered by affine schemes in the following sense. The reader may prove the following theorem.
Theorem VI-14.A functorF: (rings)‚Üí(sets)is of the formh Y for some schemeYif and only if (1)Fis a sheaf in the Zariski topology, and (2) there exist ringsR i and open subfunctors őĪ i :h R i ‚ÜíF such that, for every fieldK, F(K)is the union of the images ofh R i (K) under the mapsőĪ i
.
As an easy application, one can use the theorem to show the existence of fibered products. The construction of fibered products in the category of schemes, which was explained in Chapter I, is of fundamental importance, 
260VI.  Schemes and Functors but it is surprisingly clumsy. Using the functorial point of view, we can at least describe the fibered product of two schemes by giving its functor of points directly. IfX‚ÜíSandY‚ÜíSare morphisms of schemes, the fibered productX√ó S Yis determined by saying that it is the scheme whose functor of points is the fibered product of functorsh X √ó h S h Y
. Its existence is established in the following exercise.
Exercise VI-15.(a)  Show that iff:A‚ÜíCandg:B‚ÜíCare mor- phisms of functors all of which are sheaves in the Zariski topology, the fibered productA√ó C Bis a sheaf in the Zariski topology.
(b) Use the open covering of the fibered product suggested in Chapter I and the above theorem to prove the existence of fibered products in the category of schemes.
The next example gives a different way of looking at maps to projec- tive spaces. Theorem III-37 may be translated immediately into our new language: Theorem VI-16.IfY=P n Z , then h Y (X)=  locally free subsheavesF‚äāO n+1 X that locally are summands of rankn  = {invertible sheavesPonXwith an epimorphismO n+1 X ‚ÜíP} {isomorphisms} , where isomorphism is defined as in Corollary III-42.

By what we have just said, a K-valued point ofP n K is a ranknsummandF‚äāK n+1 ; letLbe the quo- tientL=K n+1 /F.The Zariski tangent spaceTat this point is the set of all summandsF  ‚äā(K[őĶ]/(őĶ) 2 ) n+1 that restrict toFmoduloőĶ. We will see other examples of the use of this theorem in the next section.
The functorial point of view is developed in far greater depth and detail in Demazure and Gabriel [1970], to which the interested reader is referred for more information.
One of the principal goals in Grothendieck‚Äôs work on schemes was to find a characterization of scheme-functors by weak general properties that could often be checked in practice and so lead to many existence theorems in algebraic geometry (like Brown‚Äôs theorem in the homotopy category; see Spanier [1966, Chapter 7.7]). It seemed at first that this program would fail completely and that scheme-functors were really quite special; see Hi- ronaka [1962], for instance. Artin, however, discovered an extraordinary 
262VI.  Schemes and Functors approximation theorem exhibiting a category of functorsFonly ‚Äúa little‚ÄĚ larger than the scheme-functors that can indeed be characterized by weak general properties. Geometrically speaking, the functorsFare like spaces obtained by dividing affines by ‚Äú ŐĀetale equivalence relations‚ÄĚ and then glu- ing. He calls themalgebraic spaces(for a typical occasion where they arise, see Section VI.2.4). For details, see Artin [1971] and Knutson [1971].
VI.2.2   Parameter Spaces The Hilbert Scheme.Probably the one area where the notion of the functor of points has had the most impact is in the construction and de- scription of parameter spaces. We have already mentioned in Section III.3.3, for example, that the subschemes of a projective spaceP n K over a fieldK having a given Hilbert polynomialPform a scheme, which we will callH P
.
It seems surprising that such a statement has an unambiguous meaning.
While it is intuitively plausible that thesetof such objects might form the points of a variety, couldn‚Äôt they form a variety in several different ways?
And in what sense do they form a scheme?
The answers are obtained by making precise what properties we want the correspondence between the set of subschemes and the set of points of H P to have. Specifically, note that ifX‚äāP n K √óB‚ÜíBis any flat family of subschemes ofP n K with Hilbert polynomialP,we get a map from the points ofBwith residue fieldKto the points ofH P with residue fieldK, sending a pointb‚ąąBto the point ofH P corresponding to the fiberX b ofH P overb. It is natural to ask that this map come from a regular map B‚ÜíH P
. Carrying this a little further, we wantH P to have the property that for any schemeBoverK,the set of flat families of subscheme ofP n K with Hilbert polynomialPparametrized byBis naturally identified with the set of maps fromBtoH P
. Finally, since the problem of parametrizing subschemes ofP n K with a given Hilbert polynomial should be in some sense the same for allK,we would like to do this over SpecZ‚ÄĒ that is (as in the case of the Grassmannian, which is indeed a special case of a Hilbert scheme) define for eachPa single objectH P over SpecZsuch that for any Kthe productH P √óSpecKparametrizes subschemes ofP n K with Hilbert polynomialP.
To say this a little differently, we make the following definition: Definition VI-21.TheHilbert functorh P ‚ÄĒ called the ‚Äúfunctor of flat families of schemes inP n Z with Hilbert polynomialP‚ÄĚ ‚ÄĒ is the functor h P : (schemes) ‚ó¶ ‚Üí(sets) that associates to anyBthe set of subschemesX‚äāP n B flat overBwhose fibers over points ofBhave Hilbert polynomialP.
We then want to take the Hilbert schemeH P to be the scheme that representsh P : in other words, the scheme whose functor of points ish P
.
 VI.2 Characterization of a Space by its Functor of Points263 By Yoneda‚Äôs Lemma (VI-1), this determines the schemeH P , if one such exists; the key theorem is thus the following.
Theorem VI-22.There exists a schemeH P whose functor of points is the functorh P
.
Note that for any schemeSwe can make an analogous definition of a functorh P,S :(S‚ąíschemes) ‚ó¶ ‚Üí(sets) on the category ofS-schemes, and that ifH P is the scheme representing the functorh P as above, thenh P,S is represented by theS-schemeH P √óS.
In fact, it turns out thatH P √óSpecKis often a scheme that is genuinely nota variety. We will describe, in Exercises VI-35 through VI-37, a famous example of Mumford [1962] of a Hilbert scheme that is nonreduced even at points corresponding to nonsingular, irreducible projective varieties.
The statement of Theorem VI-22 has another interpretation that is often useful: saying that the functorh P is representable is the same thing as saying that there exists auniversal family‚ÄĒ that is, a schemeHand a subschemeX‚äāP n Z √óHflat overHwith Hilbert polynomialP‚ÄĒ such that any subschemeY‚äāP n Z √óBflat overBwith Hilbert polynomialPis equal to the fiber productY=X√ó H B‚äāP n Z √óBfor a unique morphism B‚ÜíH.Clearly, if a universal familyX‚äāP n Z √óHexists, thenH represents the functorh P
. Conversely, if a schemeHrepresentsh P , then the subschemeX‚äāP n Z √óHassociated to the identity map is universal in the above sense.
We will not give a proof of Theorem VI-22 but will indicate how it may be approached; for more details we refer the reader to Mumford [1966] or Koll ŐĀar [1996].
The idea is easy to summarize: reducing to the case of a subscheme Xof projective spaceP n B over a base of the formB=SpecRwithR a local ring, such a schemeXis determined by its idealI(X)‚äāS= R[X 0 ,...,X n ], which in turn is determined formsufficiently large (in a sense depending only onP) by its degreempieceI(X) m ‚äāS m
. Setting M=  m+n n  andq=P(m), this in turn corresponds to a point in the GrassmannianG B (q, M) parametrizing summands of codimensionP(m) in the freeR-moduleS m ‚ąľ = R M
.In this wayH P,B becomes a subscheme of the GrassmannianG B (q, M) of such planes.
The key point is that we can choose a singlemthat has this property uniformly for every subschemeXwith Hilbert polynomialP; that is, that for everyP,there is anm 0 such that ifm‚Č•m 0 andXis a subscheme of P n K with Hilbert polynomialP,thenI(X) l‚Č•m is generated byI(X) m , and the codimension ofI(X) m inS m isP X (m). Examining our proof that the Hilbert polynomial is a polynomial, we see that to prove this it is enough to show that the degrees of the generators of the free modules in the minimal free resolution ofI(X) can be bounded in terms of the Hilbert polynomial 
264VI.  Schemes and Functors ofX.This is done by using the idea ofCastelnuovo regularityofX; a more complete description would take us too far afield.
We thus have, for every flat familyX‚äāP n B over a baseB=SpecR‚ÄĒ that is, an element ofh P (SpecR) ‚ÄĒ a summand of corankqinR M ‚ÄĒ that is, an element of the setg(q, M)(R), whereg=g(q, M) is the Grassmannian functor as defined in Exercise VI-18. This association extends to a natural transformation of functors fromh ‚ąó P tog(q, M), whereh ‚ąó P : (rings)‚Üí(sets) is the functorh ‚ąó P (R)=h P (SpecR), and hence to a natural transformation from the Hilbert functorh P to the functor respresented by the Grassman- nianG=G Z (q, M).
To finish the argument, one must show that there exists a subscheme H P ‚äāGsuch that a morphismŌÜ:B‚ÜíGcomes in this way from a flat familyX‚äāP n B with Hilbert polynomialPif and only ifŌÜfactors through H P
. We will content ourselves here with describing the equations ofH P as a closed subscheme ofG.LetYbe the universal subbundle onG.We have multiplication maps mult k :Y‚äóS k ‚ÜíS k+m , whereS=Z[X 0 ,...,X n ], and we can takeH P to be the ‚Äúdeterminantal‚ÄĚ subscheme ofG Z (q, M) defined by the conditions that rank(mult k )‚Č§dimS k+m ‚ąíP(m+k) for allk‚Č•0. It is immediate that the desired mapsŌÜall factor through this subscheme. Given any pointpin this subscheme, one shows that (because mhas been chosen so large) the ideal generated by the corresponding linear subspace ofS m defines a scheme with Hilbert polynomialP.This gives us a ‚Äútautological family‚ÄĚ onH P of schemes with Hilbert polynomialP.Given any mapŌÜfrom a schemeBintoH P , one can ‚Äúpull back‚ÄĚ this family by using the fibered product to get a family overB,and the mapŌÜwill be associated to this family. Once all this is verified, the description ofH P by its functor of points ensures thatH P does not depend on the choice ofm.
Examples of Hilbert Schemes.We will mention, largely in the form of exercises, some examples of Hilbert schemes. To begin with, the Grassman- nianG S (k, n) is a Hilbert scheme: it parametrizes subschemesXof degree 1 and dimensionk(specifically, with Hilbert polynomialP(m)=  m+k k  in the projective spaceP n S
. The following exercise deals with the simplest special case of this, but the general statement (and proof) differs only nu- merically.
Exercise VI-23.LetP(m)=m+ 1 be the Hilbert polynomial of a line.
Show that the Hilbert scheme of subschemes ofP 3 Z with Hilbert polynomial Pis the Grassmannian introduced in Exercise VI-20.
Another very straightforward example is hypersurfaces. It is a standard observation that the set of hypersurfaces of degreedin projective space 
VI.2 Characterization of a Space by its Functor of Points265 P n K over a fieldKmay be identified with the points of the projective space of homogeneous polynomials of degreedinn+ 1 variables. In fact, this projective space turns out to be the Hilbert scheme of such hypersurfaces.
As in the preceding example, the following exercise deals with one typical example.
Exercise VI-24.LetP(m)=2m+ 1 be the Hilbert polynomial of a conic curve. Show that the Hilbert schemeH P of subschemes ofP 2 Z with Hilbert polynomialPisP 5 Z
.
Beyond these examples, the geometry of Hilbert schemes is much less well known. Even the Hilbert schemes parametrizing zero-dimensional sub- schemes of projective spaceP n K over a field remain mysterious: Iarrobino [1985], for example, has shown (contrary to naive expectations) that such Hilbert schemes are not in general irreducible. In the case ofP 2 K , they are in fact irreducible and nonsingular, but their global geometry presents many problems: see, for example, Collino [1988]. One case where we can actually give a description is the following exercise.
Exercise VI-25.LetPbe  the  constant  polynomial  2.  Show  that  the Hilbert schemeH P parametrizing subschemes ofP 2 Z with Hilbert poly- nomialPmay be obtained by blowing up the productP 2 Z √óP 2 Z along the diagonal and then taking the quotient by the involution exchanging factors.
In the general setting our knowledge of Hilbert schemes is minimal. For example, in the case of curves in projective 3-spaceP 3 K over a fieldK‚ÄĒ the simplest example of a Hilbert scheme parametrizing schemes that are pure positive-dimensional but not hypersurfaces ‚ÄĒ we do not have even a guess as to the number of components ofH P , their dimension, or their smoothness or singularity. For a discussion of this case, see Harris and Eisenbud [1982].
Variations on the Hilbert Scheme Construction.We have defined the Hilbert scheme parametrizing subschemes of projective space with given Hilbert polynomialP.In fact, with very little additional effort we can generalize this substantially.
The first thing to notice is that ifX‚äāP n S is any closed subscheme, we can define a functor h P,X :(S‚ąíschemes) ‚ó¶ ‚ąí‚Üí(sets) by associating to anyS-schemeBthe set of flat families of subschemes of Xwith Hilbert polynomialPoverB,that is, h P,X (B)=  X‚äāB√ó S X‚äāB√ó S P n S =P n B , flat overB, with Hilbert polynomialP 
.
The key fact, which is not hard to establish, is the following.
Exercise VI-26.Show thath P,X is a closed subfunctor ofh P
.
 266VI.  Schemes and Functors It follows that there exists a closed subschemeH P,X ‚äāH P whose func- tor of points ish P,X ; this scheme is what we call theHilbert scheme of subschemes ofXwith Hilbert polynomialP.
As an important example, if we takeP(m)=  k+m k  to be the Hilbert polynomial of ak-plane, we will see in Section VI.2.3 below that the scheme H P,X we arrive at is the Fano schemeF k (X)ofk-planes onX,as defined in Section IV.3. Nor is this just an idle observation: apart from giving a more natural definition, this characterization of the Fano schemes will allow us to determine their tangent spaces.
Now suppose we are given two projectiveS-schemesX‚äāP m S andY‚äā P n S
. We may embed the productX√ó S Yin projective space via the Segre map X√ó S Y‚ÜíP m S √ó S P n S ‚ÜíP N S , whereN=(m+ 1)(n+1)‚ąí1. We thus have Hilbert schemes parametrizing subschemes of a productX√ó S Y.
This in turn allows us to parametrize morphisms fromXtoY,by con- sidering their graphs as subschemes of the product. The two things we need to check are that (1)  the condition on a subschemeZ‚äāX√ó S Ythat the projection map ŌÄ X :Z‚ÜíXbe an isomorphism is an open condition on the Hilbert scheme of subschemes ofX√ó S Y; and (2)  the Hilbert polynomials of the graphs őď ŌÜ of morphismsŌÜ:X‚ÜíY of bounded projective degree are bounded. (See Harris [1995] for a definition of ‚Äúprojective degree‚ÄĚ.) Given this, we see that there are quasiprojective schemes parametrizing the morphisms of given degree fromXtoY,and similarly a quasiprojective scheme Isom(X, Y) parametrizing isomorphisms fromXtoY.Again, by ‚Äúparametrize‚ÄĚ we mean represent the functor isom X,Y :(S‚ąíschemes) ‚ó¶ ‚ąí‚Üí(sets) given by isom X,Y (B)={isomorphismsŌÜ:B√ó S X‚ÜíB√ó S YasB-schemes}.
We should mention one further generalization of the construction of the Hilbert scheme that is very useful in practice. This is therelative Hilbert scheme,  which parametrizes subschemes of members of a flat family of schemes.
To set this up, letSbe a scheme over a fieldK,and suppose thatX‚äāP n S is any scheme flat overS.We can then consider the functor h P,X/S :(K‚ąíschemes) ‚ó¶ ‚ąí‚Üí(sets) 
VI.2 Characterization of a Space by its Functor of Points267 that associates to anyK-schemeBthe set of flat families overBof sub- schemes of fibers ofXoverS‚ÄĒ that is, h P,X/S (B)=  pairs (őĹ,ő£) such thatőĹ:B‚ÜíSand ő£‚äāB√ó S X‚äāP n B is flat overBwith Hilbert polynomialP 
.
Once more, we can adapt our basic construction to show thath P,X/S (B) is represented by aK-schemeH P,X/S
.
Finally, we can apply the relative Hilbert scheme construction to a pair of flat families to parametrize morphisms between members of two families!
Thus, ifX‚ÜíSandY‚ÜíSare any flat families, we have a quasiprojective scheme Mor d,X/S,Y /S parametrizing morphisms of given degree from fibers ofXoverSto corresponding fibers ofY‚ÜíS,and similarly a scheme parametrizing isomorphisms.
Note that ifX‚ÜíSandY‚ÜíTare families with possibly different bases, we can parametrize morphisms from fibers ofX/Sto fibers ofY/T by pulling both back to families over the productS√óTand performing this construction there.

VI.2.3   Tangent Spaces to Schemes in Terms of Their Functors of Points Tangent Spaces to Hilbert Schemes.One facet of the Hilbert scheme that is best described in terms of its functor of points is its tangent space at a point. We first introduce the notion of a first-order deformation: ifY is any scheme andX‚äāYa closed subscheme, afirst-order deformation of XinYis defined to be a flat familyX‚äāY√óSpecK[őĶ]/(őĶ 2 ) such that the fiber ofXover the reduced point SpecK‚äāSpecK[őĶ]/(őĶ 2 )isX.It then follows, via the characterization of tangent vectors to schemes given in Section VI.1.3, thatthe tangent space to the Hilbert schemeH P at a point [X]is the space of first-order deformations inP n K ofX; and more generally, ifY‚äāP n K is a projective scheme, the tangent space to the Hilbert scheme H Y P at a point [X] is the space of first-order deformations ofXinY.
 268VI.  Schemes and Functors This is especially useful since the space of first-order deformations may often be calculated, even in circumstances where we have no hope of writing down the equations ofH P
. To do this, we introduce thenormal sheafN X/Y to a closed subschemeXof a schemeY: this is defined to be the sheaf N X/Y = Hom O X (J/J 2 ,O X ) = Hom O Y (J,O X ) whereJ=J X/Y is the ideal sheaf ofXinY.We then have the following basic theorem.
Theorem VI-29.Given a closed subschemeXof a schemeY,the space of first-order deformations ofXinYis the space of global sections of its normal sheafN X/Y
.
Proof.To begin with, letX‚äāY√óSpecK[őĶ]/(őĶ 2 ) be any subscheme whose intersection with the fiberY ‚ąľ = Y√óSpecK‚äāY√óSpecK[őĶ]/(őĶ 2 )isX(do not assumeXis flat). LetU‚äāYbe any affine open subset,V=X‚ą©Uthe corresponding affine open subset ofX,andV=X‚ą©(U√óSpecK[őĶ]/(őĶ 2 )).

One trivial but useful consequence of this theorem is the following.
Corollary VI-30.The dimension of any irreducible componentő£of the Hilbert scheme is at most the dimension of the space of sections of the normal sheaf of any schemeXwith[X]‚ąąő£.
In fact, this a priori estimate for the dimension of a component of the Hilbert scheme gives the right answer more often than not, especially when applied to a general point [X] of a component ofH P
. The following exer- cises give examples of this.

Tangent Spaces to Fano Schemes.One particularly nice example of schemes that are in many ways best characterized by their functor of points are the Fano schemesF k (X)‚äāG S (k, n) of a schemeX‚äāP n S described in Section IV.3. We will see, for example, how the description given in Section VI.1.3 of the tangent spaces to a functor allows us to compute the Zariski tangent spaces to a Fano scheme much more readily that we could from the explicit equations introduced in Section IV.3; this will in turn allow us to say in many cases whether a linear space őõ‚äāXcorresponds to a nonsingular or a singular point ofF k (X). (For the following discussion, we will introduce some notation: for a planeW‚äāK n+1 and the corresponding linear subspace őď‚äāP n K , we‚Äôll write őď = [W] andW=  ŐÉ őď.) The characterization of Fano schemes is straightforward: we may de- fine the Fano schemeF k (X) of a subschemeX‚äāP n S to be simply the Hilbert schemeH P,X of subschemes ofXwith Hilbert polynomialP(m)=  k+m k  ‚ÄĒ that is, the functor f k (X):(S‚ąíschemes) ‚ó¶ ‚ąí‚Üí(sets) that associates to anyS-schemeBthe set of families ofk-planes contained inX√ó S B,that is, f k (X)(B)=  ő£‚äāB√ó S X‚äāB√óP n S =P n B , flat overB, such that ő£ b ‚äāP n őļ(b) is ak-plane for allb‚ąąB 

As promised, the characterization of the Fano scheme by its functor of points allows us to determine its tangent spaces readily, and in particular to give us criteria for the smoothness and/or singularity of Fano schemes.

As an application, consider the simplest possible case, the Fano scheme F 1 (S)‚äāG K (1,3) =G K (2,4) of lines on a surfaceS‚äāP 3 K
. LetL‚äāS be a line, which we will assume is not contained in the singular locus ofS (if it is, the Fano schemeF 1 (S) will have four-dimensional tangent space atL‚ÄĒ in other words, it will be very singular!). To a general pointp‚ąąL, then, we may associate the projective tangent space 1 T p S, which will be a plane inP 3 K containingL; thus we get a (rational) map ő≥:L‚ąí‚ÜíP(K 4 /  ŐÉ L) ‚ąľ = P 1 K
.
This map is given by the partial derivatives of the defining polynomial ofS, so that ifSis nonsingular alongL‚ÄĒ in other words, if these partials have no common zeroes ‚ÄĒ the degree ofő≥will bed‚ąí1. IfShas any singular points onL, conversely, it will be less.
In fact, the degree of the mapő≥is precisely what determines the di- mension  of  the  tangent  space  toF 1 (S)atL.  This  is  simple  to  see:  if ŌÜ:  ŐÉ L‚ÜíK 4 /  ŐÉ Lis any homomorphism, the induced map  ŐĄŌÜ:L‚ąí‚ÜíP(K 4 /  ŐÉ L) ‚ąľ = P 1 K p‚ąí‚Üí[ŌÜ( ŐÉp)+  ŐÉ L] will have degree 1 ifŌÜhas rank 2 and degree 0 ifŌÜhas rank 1. Thus, ifő≥has degree deg(ő≥)‚Č•2, the tangent space toF 1 (S)atLwill be zero- dimensional; ifő≥has degree 1 it will be one-dimensional; and ifő≥is constant it will be two-dimensional. We get the following corollaries: ‚ÄĘThe Fano scheme of lines on a nonsingular quadric surface inP 3 K is nonsingular, while the Fano scheme of lines on a quadric cone is every- where nonreduced.
 274VI.  Schemes and Functors ‚ÄĘMore generally, the Fano scheme of the cone inP 3 K over any plane curve is nonreduced.
‚ÄĘThe Fano scheme of lines on a nonsingular surface of degreed‚Č•3in P 3 K consists of isolated reduced points.
There is a further corollary dependent  on a further fact about Fano schemes of hypersurfaces: ifX‚äāP n B is any hypersurface of degreed‚ÄĒ that is, a closed subscheme ofP n B , flat overS,whose fiber over each pointb‚ąąB is a hypersurface of degreedinP n őļ(b) ‚ÄĒ then the Fano schemeF k (X)‚äā G B (k+1,n+1) ofk-planes onXis flat over the open subset ofBwhere it has the expected fiber dimension (k+ 1)(n‚ąík)‚ąí  k+d k 
. SinceF k (X) may be described as the zero locus of a section of a locally free sheaf on G B (kn), this is a generalization of the fact that complete intersections are flat, but it is beyond the scope of this book. Given it, however, we have the following consequence: ‚ÄĘAll cubic surfaces that contain only finitely many lines contain the same number of lines, properly counted; that is, their Fano schemes all have degree 27.
In general, our description of the tangent spaces to the Fano scheme F k (X) allows us to determine the dimension ofF k (X) at a point őõ in terms of the normal bundle to the plane őõ‚äāX,but it is rare that this will be determined by the singularities ofXalong őõ. In fact, as soon as we get to hypersurfacesX‚äāP n K withn‚Č•4, we see examples to the contrary.

VI.2.4   Moduli Spaces A similar situation arises when we want to construct a moduli space of geometric objects. For example, we would like to identify the set of non- singular, projective curves of genusgover a fieldKwith the set of closed points of a ‚Äúmoduli scheme‚ÄĚM g
. To avoid unnecessary complication, we restrict to the case char(K) = 0. Again, the way to express what we want is to introduce thefunctor of nonsingular curves of genusg: this is the functor M fun g :(K‚ąíschemes) ‚ó¶ ‚Üí(sets) that assigns to any schemeBoverKthe set of flat morphismsŌÄ:X‚ÜíB whose fibers are nonsingular curves of genusg, up to isomorphismX ‚ąľ = X  asB-schemes. We defineM g to be the scheme (if any) that represents the functorM fun g
.
Since schemes are uniquely determined by their functors of points, the only difficulty with the ‚Äúdefinitions‚ÄĚ above is whether such schemes exist ‚ÄĒ 
VI.2 Characterization of a Space by its Functor of Points275 that is, whether the given functors are representable. The answer in the case ofH P is yes; and indeed, as we have pointed out, the characterization of H P as the scheme that represents the functorh P is crucial to the proof of existence.
However, in the case ofM g , the answer is no! For example, even in the case of nonsingular curves of genus 1 overCthere does not exist a moduli spaceM 1 in this sense. To see this, it suffices to show that there does not exist a universal family ‚ÄĒ in other words, a flat morphismŌÄ:C‚ÜíMof schemes with fiber nonsingular curves of genus 1 such that for every family Y‚ÜíBof nonsingular curves of genus 1 there are unique mapsŌÜ:B‚ÜíM and ő¶ :Y‚ÜíCforming a fiber product diagram: Y ő¶  C B ő∑  ŌÜ  M ŌÄ  In fact, there does not even exist a tautological family ‚ÄĒ that is, a morphism C‚ÜíMand a bijection between the closed points ofMand the set of isomorphism classes of nonsingular curves of genus 1 such that the fiber over each pointp‚ąąMis in the isomorphism class corresponding to the pointp. We will exhibit two kinds of obstructions to the existence of a universal family, one local and one global.
For the local obstruction, recall that curves of genus 1 overCare classified by theirj-invariant: we can write any such curve as the plane cubic y 2 =x(x‚ąí1)(x‚ąíőĽ) for some complexőĽ=0,1; and two such curvesC őĽ andC őĽ  will be iso- morphic if and only if theirj-invariantsj(őĽ) andj(őĽ  ) are equal, where j(őĽ) = 256¬∑ (őĽ 2 ‚ąíőĽ+1) 3 őĽ 2 (őĽ‚ąí1) 2 ; see Silverman [1986, Chapter III, Proposition 1.7], for example. It can be shown that given a familyX‚ÜíBof nonsingular curves of genus 1 with nonsingular baseB,the functionjis a regular function onB; and locally around any pointb‚ąąB, őĽcan be defined as a regular function, too (though it is not unique). It follows that if there did exist a tautological family, there would have to exist one with base the affine lineA 1 C with coordinatej. But no such family can exist, because at the pointj= 0 the functionőĽ 2 ‚ąí őĽ+ 1 would vanish, and thusjwould vanish triply. Less obviously, because j  (‚ąí1) = 0, it also follows thatjcan only assume the value 1728 with even multiplicity. (Note that the valuesj= 0 and 1728 correspond to the elliptic curves with ‚Äúextra automorphisms‚ÄĚ ‚ÄĒ that is, whose automorphism groups contain the automorphism group of a general elliptic curve as subgroups of index 3 and 2, respectively.) 
276VI.  Schemes and Functors Turning to the global obstruction, even if a tautological family exists over a varietyMwhose points correspond to isomorphism classes of curves, such a family may not be universal; that is, it may not induce a bijection between families over a baseBand maps ofBtoM.For example, if we simply exclude the curves ofj-invariant 0 and 1728 ‚ÄĒ that is, consider the functor of families of nonsingular curves of genus 1 not isomorphic toC 0 orC 1728 ‚ÄĒ we might hope that the puncturedj-lineM=A 1 C ‚ąí{0,1728}would be a moduli space; and indeed, a tautological family does exist over this open subset ofA 1
. It is not universal, however: for example, for any fixedőĽ, let B  be any variety with fixed-point-free involutionŌĄ, and consider the family overB=B  /ŌĄformed by taking the quotient of the productE√óBby the involution őĻ:((x, y),p)‚Üí((x,‚ąíy),ŌĄ(p)) This is a family all of whose fibers are isomorphic toC őĽ , and so it can only come from the constant mapB‚ÜíM; but it can be shown that the family itself is not trivial.
It is the presence of automorphisms ofC őĽ that is responsible for this phe- nomenon. Indeed, an analogous argument shows that we can never have a moduli space for schemes modulo isomorphism when some of the objects to be parametrized admit automorphisms. This explains also the discrep- ancy between the notions of tautological and universal family: in the case of the Hilbert schemeHparametrizing subschemes ofP n K if two families X,X  ‚äāP n K √ó K Bover a varietyBcorrespond to the same mapB‚ÜíH it follows that they are equal fiber by fiber and hence equal. By contrast, in the case of a moduli space, it would follow only that they are isomorphic fiber by fiber; if the fibers admitted automorphisms, those isomorphisms would not be unique and so might not fit together to give an isomorphism X ‚ąľ = X 
.
How do we deal with these difficulties? The most naive (and least sat- isfactory) way is simply to exclude all schemes with automorphisms from consideration when trying to construct a moduli space. This works in some contexts: for example, since the family of curves of genusgwith automor- phisms has in a suitable sense codimensiong‚ąí2 among all curves, if we are concerned in particular with the divisor theory of the moduli space of curves of genusg‚Č•4, we can afford to look just at the moduli spaceM 0 g of automorphism-free nonsingular curves of genusg, which does exist.
There are two more serious approaches, both of which are in active use.
The first is to takeM g to be the scheme whose functor of points ‚Äúmost closely approximates‚ÄĚM fun g
. It turns out that there is such a thing, called acoarse moduli space, and that it has nice properties; for example, the value of its functor of points at an algebraically closed fieldKis really the set of isomorphism classes of nonsingular curves over it. The second way out is to enlarge the category of schemes in a different way, to the category ofalgebraic stacks. A discussion of this would take us too far, so we‚Äôll just 
VI.2 Characterization of a Space by its Functor of Points277 refer the reader to Vistoli [1989, Appendix] for a short treatment and to Behrend et al. [‚Č•2001] for a full treatment; see also Mumford [1965] for an introduction to the functorial point of view on moduli spaces.
